RATING: 7
An interesting coop.  Players play cards to advance the group along various tracks.  All the while players need to balance their increasing corruption against Sauron advances along the corruption track.  The players win if at least the ring bearer survives and successfully destroys the ring.

The players definitely need to work together in order to succeed.  Because of the different character powers, certain players will be better equipped to handle certain obstacles.  Careful orchestration of the group's progression is vital, particularly as the ring bearer must be protected.  Against that, the game provides a series of dangers that become increasingly more difficult and carry and increasingly harsher penalties.  The progression works out very nicely as the difficulty ramps up towards the end of the game.

POSITIVES:  Lots of theme.  If you like LotR, chances are you will like this game.  The coop elements work well -- players *must* work together in order to succeed.

NEGATIVES:  I don't feel a strong connection between the game mechanics and the theme.  It seems like someone said "Hey, it would be cool if we made a LotR board game!  What mechanics sound good?"  I'm sure players can connect the mechanics and theme if they really want to, but to me the mechanics aren't particularly evocative.

A great coop game.  Strongly recommended.  I would gladly play this one again.

RATING: 6.5
[i]2017/05/15 - 2 plays 2-4 players - 0 victories[/i]

Lord of the Rings has a very strong gameplay, but is quite abstract, which can make it more difficult to bring to the table. But when it does, it creates a great tension, and lots of difficult and interesting choices. I specially like that you will sometimes have to sacrifice yourself to help the fellowship, which I find quite thematic. This player elimination can however be a problem for some, even though you can still participate in the discussion.

The frequent use of dice also increases the feeling of radomness compared to Pandemic, so your enjoyement will depend on how you cope with dice-induced randomness. I lost on Mount Doom because of a unlucky roll, but it was an exciting everybody stands up to watch moment.

Difficulty can be easily adjusted, but I have the feeling that at 2-player the original rules are really punishing, I need to confirm this with more plays.

The playing boards are very beautiful, and variability comes from token draw. A pity that events are fixed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3
A very well-themed game. To those who say it isn't, I challenge you to take the identical mechanics and apply a different theme to them!

Very, very tense, and knowing whether you've won or not might be down to the wire. I like that players are frequently willing to take a hit for the team, and even die: I can't think of any other cooperative that has this element.

I'm very surprised at how low LotR is rated. Most people say it's because it's an "older" game, and can't compare with the "newer" cooperatives; but it's considerably better than Pandemic, and considerably less abstract! For a real nightmare, play it with five players!

Too bad about the rulebook, though....
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I just don't like cooperative games. All my gaming buddies love this game. I am the only one who doesn't. The cooperation in this game seems minimal to me. For the most part, the newbies cannot contribute anything since the experienced players will tell them what to do. Even if all the players were experienced, the decisions that the players had to make involved no strategy or tactics. The decisions involved a minimal calculation of the odds. The game just seemed incredibly boring luck dependent game where depending on the draw of the tile you might or might not escape a particular board.  I just don't see any meaningful cooperation. It's like using four (# of players) brains to do a job one can do. There is no private information or personal talent that we bring to the table. Any one person who can see everyone's cards can arrive at the same decision which all the players invariably arrive at. There is no synergy, there is no welfare improvement. What a waste of my time. The game would function just as smoothly if I was comatose and one of my teammates played my cards for me. A genuine cooperative experience is when everyone has an input, like in a team sport or in research. Playing these kinds of games are like going for a movie with friends. The theme may be fun and we're there together but it's not a much of a group accomplishment. I suspect that most people enjoy the game because of the theme. If we changed the theme to one which they didn't enjoy then the game's rating would plummet.

RATING: 5
It's a very abstract card game where you play four different symbols, and that's almost all there is. The progression of events and special card distributions adds some interesting tension that follows the "story" of Lord of the Rings. But they don't really change the gameplay at all.

You are invited to "feel" the theme of LOTR in the game's narrative arc. And it can be fun if players build the narrative themselves as they play. But I can't rate the game itself highly for doing that. It merely provides the opportunity for creative storytelling on top of some very light and dry gameplay.

RATING: 3
I'm always a little dubious about games (or anything else)that sell themselves on the back of an already proven masterpiece. Regrettably, this is another case of well meant intentions falling short.
I'm all in favour of playing co-operatively, which indeed we did; but despite our best efforts, we were subject both to the cards we drew, and the cards that turned up against us. When you've worked as a team, made it through to the final board with relative ease, only to find the cards crapping all over you at the last moment, the question has to be "Why did we bother ?". We did nothing wrong, and there was nothing that we could have done to prevent the seemingly inevitable slaughter (four really awful cards in a row).
In short, too much chance, too little control. Nice art, shame about the game.

RATING: 7
I am a fan of cooperative games, but dislike Pandemic for some reason. 

This game appeals to me thematically, although it is literally a disguised euro game. That said, I love the experience it provides, as well as its overall simplicity. 

Unlike in other coop games where certain characters may be overpowered, this game distributes your group's resources and literally forces you to make sacrifices as a group in overcoming some of the obstacles. As a result, my partner and I love the feeling when we manage to beat the game together.

RATING: 6
10 co-op plays without expansions. *See Plays for details. LOTR is basically a "social" game.  I normally prefer playing strategy games, but I find myself in the mood to play this "choose your own adventure" more often than one would expect.  As for the gameplay, the luck of the draw and die rolls heavily determines your score, but it also adds intensity and excitement.  Although playing with an expansion is preferable, I continue to play and enjoy the base game on occasion.  I recommend using only 1 expansion at a time. Playing with more than one expansion adds considerable length to the game!  We usually play with Sauron on space 15.  We really only use Watchful Peace and The One Ring with the Sauron expansion.

I much prefer playing Lord of the Rings with 2 players or solo, in some cases having players control more than 1 Hobbit (unless we're using the Sauron expansion.)  My reasoning is that Merry or Pippen may not even get a turn until the second or third scenario board.  Also, there can be way too many opinions as to what to do, which can slooow down the game.   


[b]Friends & Foes[/b] 
25 co-op Plays!  I really enjoy starting with Bree as the first scenario.  The additional features add more flexibility for the Fellowship.  

[b]Sauron[/b]
5 Plays!  Intense fun, but also provides an overwhelming feeling of doom and despair.  Much harder than the base game!


[b]Dark Events[/b] 
2 solo plays with 2 Hobbits using only the Base game without expansions!  Enjoyable, but now even more difficult to overcome the Events in Mordor.       


[b]Battlefields[/b]
4 solo plays and 3 co-op plays!  At the moment I prefer the Battlefield combat to the card removal combat found in Friends & Foes.

RATING: 9
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Spiel des Jahres Special Award: Literary Game 2001[/SIZE][/COLOR]

[i]Posted: 8/5/09, Rated: 10[/i]
{2P: ???, 3P: 10, 4P: 10, 5P: ???}

[i]One game to rule them all...[/i]

Let me start by saying this: If you're not a fan of the books and/or the movies, then you are not going to like this game.  If you do enjoy those other mediums, then you will enjoy this.  And if you love the story as I do, then you will be amazed by this game.

[i]The Lord of the Rings[/i] is a cooperative game.  That is to say, all of the players are working together to defeat "the game."  My family enjoys these games as a welcome alternative: we've played [i][thing=15062]Shadows over Camelot[/thing][/i] and [i][thing=30549]Pandemic[/thing][/i].  It was surprising for me to realize that [i]Lord of the Rings[/i] preceded both.  In fact, it is quite apparent to me that its composition of having a main board and then separate scenario boards was heavily leveraged by [i][thing=15062]Shadows[/thing][/i].  Ditto the idea of having a draw deck of tiles that advances the game elements (what [i][thing=15062]Shadows[/thing][/i] would later refer to as "The Progression of Evil").

[person=2]Knizia[/person]-bashers love to say that his games consist of dry mechanics with [geekurl=/wiki/page/Glossary#toc135]pasted-on themes[/geekurl].  That objection goes sideways for me, as I think almost every game can be reduced to those ingredients.  What makes this game so incredible is the richness and depth of the source material, and the effectiveness with which it has been distilled down to a 60-minute gaming experience.  You feel the enormity of the evil that is arrayed against you.  The Big Eye is an imposing figure as it marches down the corruption track towards your hobbits.

Each scenario has its own personality.  Take "Helm's Deep" for example: if the players can rush to complete the "Friendship" line and establish an alliance with Rohan before the second event, then they're off to a good start.  If they can then rush through the "Travelling" line so that the Riders of Rohan reinforce Helm's Deep before the third event, then they will be well-positioned to successfully complete the scenario.  The sense of urgency and the sequencing of these plot lines map well to the literary events, which I appreciate.

This seemingly complex game structure is delivered with a spartan set of components.  The artwork of the game is original and immersive.  This is a game that is easy to teach, and perhaps even more importantly is easy to set up.  It is difficult to win, and by including a scoring mechanism there is motivation to win [i]well[/i].  [i]The Lord of the Rings[/i] was a landmark achievement in 2000, and it still represents a triumph of game design nine years later.

[i]Updated: 7/18/10, Rated: 9[/i]

[i]Lord of the Rings[/i] is an amazing feat of literary adaptation.  If I'm being objective then the one item that provides me with a tiny lingering doubt is how this it favors people who have read the books.  A "10" should have near-universal appeal.  A "9" is still a heavy hitter, and [i]LOTR[/i] will still have plenty of magic for enthusiasts.

I thought this game was a solid component of my collection but I'll have to re-assess that (especially as a gateway game that includes player elimination).

RATING: 7
I'm impressed with this cooperative game.  The replayability seems like it could be an issue, but playing it once or twice a year seems good.  So far, after 3 tries, my wife and I are really enjoying this game and still have not won.

In the first play, we started Sauron on 15 and mis-played one rule.  That is, we forced ourselves to only play cards of the same symbol as was flipped on the story tile.  We made it to Mordor, but barely.

In the second play, I believe we had all the rules straight.  We put on the ring a few times (which we didn't do the first time through) and it helped.  We started Sauron on 14 and made it near the top of Mt. Doom.  I put on the ring to, hopefully, climb the last 4 spaces.  Unfortunately, I rolled the discard 2 cards symbol on the die and had no cards.  So, I met my demise and we lost (since I had the ring).

In the third play, we really zipped through Moria and Helm's Deep.  Shelob's Lair was a bit tougher, but we weren't too close to Sauron (started on 14) so we were feeling good.  The events in Mt. Doom took a toll on us and Sam (who had the ring) was unable to discard 2 cards on an event so he fell and we lost.  Once again, our loss was due to not being able to discard cards.

After 3 consecutive losses, we won our fourth play in a resounding victory.  It wasn't even close.  We had tons of Runes and were quite a distance from Sauron.  We were shocked at how easily we won.  Sauron started on 14 again... guess it's time to move him to 12 at game start.

RATING: 5
We got this after I read the design comments by Knizia in a game design book, thought it would be interesting, even though I'm pretty skeptical of the fun-value of co-op games these days.  We were really hoping for a game that redeemed our even greater distrust of Knizia, but... redemption denied!  We played 5 games of this, 3 or 4 at the beginner level, and only came close to winning once!  This thing is next to impossible to win - at the very least, it is VERY difficult, and I can't really see too many strategies for doing better.  Hold onto your cards and try to make them last - duh!  But beyond that, it seems like the play is very tilted toward chance, and I'm pretty ambivalent about the abstracted nature of the goings-on.  Nice components, love the idea of multiple boards, there is a lot to like - we just never had any fun.  Can this thing be won?  We're sick of trying.  Knizia, you're killing us here.

RATING: 6
I enjoyed my time with this game before other co-ops existed, but it got pretty left by the wayside once Pandemic, etc., arrived on the scene. And there's a good reason for that: it's not particularly good.

The care with which the events of the novels are depicted is quite nice. Despite being mechanically unthematic the flavor text and board art manage to be extremely evocative... at least for the Tolkien enthusiast like me.

Less evocative is the card and token art which are exceptionally ugly. And the ring is atrocious as well. I'd rather have it NOT fit around the Hobbit figurines and look like an actual ring. Ugh.

Mechanically, though... the main strategy seems to be to have played the game before and remember what cards you will need later. It's normal to have an advantage in any kind of content-based game due to familiarity with said content, but this seems to be the whole strategy, which is just poor design.

There can be moments of nice tension, especially toward the end, but the game feels weirdly all-or-nothing about hurting you. Because of the way event tiles work, you're likely to get a string of terrible flips you can do nothing about (tangent: the tiles are irritating to shuffle between games. Should have been cards) and just get wrecked, OR you'll have a few turns in a row where nothing bad really happens. There isn't the slow build of Bad that happens in games like Pandemic or Ghost Stories, so it ends up feeling like it's harder to plan for, and sometimes feels more unfair on an emotional level, even though that's not necessarily actually the case.

Also, I find it odd that one of the first things you have to do is discard your cards or harm yourself. Starting out with something so punishing... I feel like if I had to take the punishment ever, I would just want to restart because it's the very beginning so why not?

The one thing I do like about the game mechanically is the Gandalf cards. Looking at those vs. the board events is the one thing that feels like it allows you to do some actual planning, instead of just hoping you draw the right cards.

All in all, this is a game I'm pretty much always down to play, but would almost never suggest over Pandemic/Ghost Stories/etc.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.6
I know Steve will give me crap for rating this so low.  The choices in this game are really uninteresting.  Should I play a tree or weapons?  Who gives a crap?  There is very little choice when your hand size is small, and the random things in the game can crush you.  Moving 1-3 spaces on the board is small compared to the bad things that can happen to you.  The bad things are there to make the game an exercise in perfectly modeling a gaussian success rate.  It's a mechanical bell curve that only needs the players to move cards around and nothing else.  There is so much randomness and chaos in the game, that I really felt as if the game would play itself if you set it up and just left it sit on the table.  I didn't feel as if I was cooperating or even really playing anything out of my own wishes.  At least in Shadows over Camelot, you can choose your quest, you can choose how to hurt the group, and there is a player playing against you.  This game is like playing a card game against a computer.  Draw well, win, draw poorly, lose and have no fun doing either one.

Oh, did I mention that I hate The Lord of the Rings movies, and everything the commercialization has sprouted?  Ten years ago, you would hear about somebody reading the story and how good it was....Ok.  You like fantasy books...I have no problem with that.  Now the overhyped and poorly acted and directed movies come out, and suddenly everything has the name Lord of the Rings attached to it.  Even mechanical Knizia games that could fit any theme from Ultimate Fighting Championships to brain surgery.  I liked it better when everything was in Egypt.  The game would still suck.  I would just have more respect for it. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The best game ever made that is completely impossible to win. Sauron will own you the first thirty times you play, no exceptions. Near-flawless game mechanics coupled with high production values make this game sweet. Those Germans, they know their games. John Howe's board illustrations are some of the best I've ever seen in a game. Cooperative element is loads of fun, strategy is deep (though a fair amount of luck for the tiles is needed). Makes you scrutinize the box, wondering "Is this Ravensberger?"

RATING: 7.5
I have owned this game since it was originally published by Hasbro before FFG bought it.  I got it because it said "Lord of the Rings" on it.  

What an incredible game.  The mechanics are excellent, and there is a lot of depth to the game.  There is also a high level of tension as Sauron continually moves closer to the left-hand side of the Corruption track, and I can see how important it is for ALL of the players to cooperate throughout the entire game.

An excellent game, and one of Knizia's best.  Will continue to update as I increase my plays and introduce the game to new players.  I have still to play only with my daughter, but the concept seems so finely tuned that I think it can be easy to introduce to the rest of the players in my group.

Another great Knizia game.  I know that some have criticized RK for rehashing the same theme over and over, just pasted onto a different box.  Well, I own only 4 of his games (and will most likely add T&E as a 5th), and none of them are the same at all.  I guess if you don't buy all 250  of his games, the "repitition" factor is diminished.

RATING: 7
Comparing this one with LotR LCG:
I've played LotR LCG more than 50 times since 2011 July.  I made my own prototyping version with high resolution images covering almost most of the expansions.  Basically, I was so eager to buy it when Siu Mui was in stock in late 2011.  However, I gave up and threw away part of my prototyping version (actually, some of them were turned to another games prototyping). Frankly speaking, the mechanism of FFG LCG version card games are very similar.  FFG used the same formula to put the theme into the game.  The creativity of the FFG LCG is not any earth shaking design.  But the quality and the graphics of the game may give you some eye catching bonus.  If you have no or little experience about FFG LCG, the LotR LCG can give you a good choice.  It is also good to try no matter you play it with your playmates or play it in solitaire mode.  

The scenarios of LotR LCG are quite difficult/ challenge. The game may give you some frustrations.  Remembering that I was so crazy that making  9 copies of Gandalfs in order to get rid of some scenarios.  I felt so annoying if I'd to order the cards individually or ordered the 2nd copies of the games in order to get my own custom piles of deck building cards.  For buying this game, my feeling was something like my nose was being picked by the FFG.  Again, I feel the game is not very creative and the game cannot capture the theme very well. 

On the other hand, I like the  LotR (Reiner Knizia) version.  This game was introduced by my friend and we'd played 2 weeks ago.  I've overlooked this game before.  After watching the play through video, I'd ordered the FFG Sliver Line Edition from the Book Depositary with HK$240.  I've played 3 times solo and 2 times with my wife.  The games simulates the theme and the story very well.  For example, the ring bearer can put on the ring and becomes invisible, the ring bearer also has higher risk of being seek by Sauron.  I cannot say this game has a very high replayability but I can say the game has a very good thematic simulation.  Although the strategic is not very deep, the level of rules balancing and its simplicity of game flow are very elegant.  You may ask yourself what is a good game?  For me, a good game something like reading a story book.  If the story book or a board game can give you a wide scope of imagination and bring you to the world of it ..then it is a good board game!  That's why, LotR  (Reiner Knizia) is a very good game for me! 

Recently I've made the expansion of the game - Friend and Foes.  I've just tried it in solitaire mode today.  Pretty good as well.  Unfortunately, FFG will not publish Friend and Foes expansion for the Silver Line Edition, otherwise, the Friends and Foes is also in my "must buy" list!

This game is suitable for light solo gamers and family players who love the story of LotR.
Just my 2 cents!

RATING: 5
A strange cooperative solitaire game, yet worthy for fans of the LotR story as it captures the feel of the books quite well. I admit, the mechanics of this could have been fitted to any number of different themes, but the job that was done on it was so thorough, from the art to the items, that it ends up being very evocative. The cooperative aspect which makes this game very hit or miss for most people certainly worked with my group, but perhaps too well, there was little tension on the part of anyone to stay in the game, everyone was completely focused on the group, which makes the game much easier. The first game we lost horribly due to playing several rules wrong, the second we won with flying colours, and beyond that I have had no real desire to play the game again. I originally bought Friends & Foes to ramp up the difficulty and add variety to the game, but the game ultimately ended up languishing on the shelf. It's not bad per se, but no one was requesting it, and I certainly wasn't enthusiastic enough about it to bring it to the table again. I don't know if the cooperative play ultimately works for me as a mechanic, as I kept having the niggling feeling that we weren't playing a game so much as trying to crack a puzzle...I just like the constant back and forth of interactive, competitive games I s'pose. Sold.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
(Revision 3- March 2007)This is now the third time I've rewritten my comment on Knizia's LOTR- back in 2001 I thought this was an amazing design and was pretty sure it was a classic for the ages.  By 2004, after playing several times in 2-3 player sessions with crappy groups and folks that never really cottoned to the cooperative aspect my opinion on it waned, particularly as I started to realize that there was a sharp disconnect between the level of theme present in the game as a whole and the actual actions taken as part of the process of playing the game.  In short, I felt (and still feel) that there is a not entirely congruous joining of a theme/narrative rich metagame and presentation with a minimalist, abstracted cardplay/board movement system that never really felt like LOTR to me.  Here we are in 2007, and just a couple of weeks ago I said in my interview with Steve Weeks that I didn't like the game and didn't get how it was regarded as so thematic.  With the release of BATTLEFIELDS, I decided to give the game another shot and I was pleased to see that my initial awe of the design returned.  LOTR is truly a masterpiece of game design, whether you like the cooperative and abstracted elements or not.  There is a HUGE degree of thematic specificity in terms both broad and subtle that really transcends the simpler on-the-board mechanics.  It's not about moving the stupid cone forward.  It's about a lot of concepts that work as mechanics between players rather than between the players and the system- working together to plan for inevitable disasters, waiting for the slower team members to catch up, solving problems as a team, sacrifice, and standing together at the brink of annihilation.  Things like that are definitely part of the LOTR theme, and they are definitely in this game.  I think it's interesting that this division between the specific and the abstract is illustrated in the artwork as well- the life tokens for example are pretty generic, but the John Howe artwork is fantasy art at its absolute best.  So an about-face?  Pretty much.  My criticisms are still valid, but as a piece of game design and as a fairly avant-garde gaming experience, there's little doubt that LOTR is a classic game that will almost certainly stand the test of time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Lord of the Rings is, to my knowledge, the first cooperative title. It took a very long time for this central idea to catch root in the gaming industry; probably because of the very strong coupling between subject (the story of Tolkien) to the mechanics (cooperation). Nowadays we have Pandemic, Ghost Stories, and quite a few others, but that didn't use to be the case. 

As in all cooperative games, you play together against the game, in this case to destroy the One Ring before Sauron corrupts all the Hobbits of the (changed) Fellowship. You work your way through 4 scenarios, each highlighting a section of the story. You are attacked from all sides, and will have to discard many valuable items and resources to survive. You will also have to make choices because you can't do everything. All in all the story is very accurately followed, leading to a strong sense of actually playing it yourself. Unfortunately the resource management pushes back some of the magic: you're not playing the Aragorn-card, but a card with two jokers on it. But it's not a big deal. What is a big deal is the fact that the randomisation of the event chips can screw you over bigtime. If you are unlucky, you hit Bad News upon Bad News, ending the game before you have had an opportunity to build up some defense. The game can be over on the second board, but equally well on the fourth, and this variation beyond the players' control diminishes the fun. This is a well-known problem of coops, and none of them truly solves the issue to satisfaction (although Pandemic comes close). Still, this game is an interesting title to play once every while.

[b]Update[/b] With the passing of the Fell... hrm, excuse me... waning interest in the LotR-franchise this game, including its dark shadow of untimely drawn event chips, never got played anymore. I was able to sell both it and its two expansions off in one sale. Others will find the journey more interesting.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.75
Excellent cooperative game - played many times. My only reservation is that although the Middle-Earth theme is there the game still feels a little abstract.

Is improved by the Friends and Foes expansion. The new LCG is coming close to a replacement for this for me as it includes many of the same elements - as well as the theme.

RATING: 10
Players are all cooperating as hobbits in the fellowship and the mission is to destroy the One Ring. The players must navigate their way across the activity tracks of four scenario boards to reach Mount Doom. Boards are exited by completing the main activity track (good), or letting the game events take over (bad!). Movement and events are handled by giving up precious resources - cards, shields and life tokens. All the way through the game there is a ticking clock in the form of the corruption track - with Sauron at one end and the hobbits down the other. As the game progresses, Sauron and the hobbits advance towards each other and if they meet, well, it's not good! An amazing, groundbreaking game that unfortunately some will loathe. Very tunable too - play competitively, even with a corrupt hobbit victory conditions!  I thoroughly enjoy playing solitaire with two hobbits against the game engine.  

2011 Update - I have played the recent Silver Line smaller edition from Fantasy Flight.  It's good, nice, compact and portable.  However, it's been "Fantasy Flighted", which means a a step backwards graphically, with a overwrought rulebook.  The icons are too indistinct, especially on the event track - you really have to squint to see the text properly.  The brown/grey cards are hard to tell apart too.  There appear to be some rules changes and/or oversights - Lembas now allows discards before drawing, and you get the full complement of Rivendell/Lothlorien cards in a two player game.  Good to see it back in a portable form, but get the original for the better components (and save your eyesight).

Still, amazing game that has seen many clones since it was published.  Lost count of the number of average cooperative "make progress, do evil" games that have come down the road since this appeared in 2000.

RATING: 8

It's all about economizing of resources while under relentless time pressure.  There also aren't many cooperative games to choose from, but that style of play works very well here.  It only gets better with the expansions, and with Sauron you can mix it up head to head.  While the basic progression of the game is similar every time, circumstances continually create new challenges.  This game has been an amazing and memorable experience every time so far.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great cooperative game.  There really isnt much room for players to play individually and you dont really feel like an individual character when playing, but it works really well as a randomized multi-player cooperative puzzle/game with fun group discussion, interaction, decisions, and comraderie in overcoming obstacles.  The game is really well designed to encourage cooperation and social interaction, e.g., a player absolutely needs to consult with everyone so that the game boards can be completed, and you usually need other players' cards to finish an objective, so everyone feels like they can contribute.  

The game should not be played, however, with over-analytical players or players who do not enjoy the theme. You definitely do not want to play with people who dislike working with others or don't like accepting suggestions. And it's more fun if everyone has about the same experience level with the game.  It might overall be a little too abstract, in usual Knizia fashion: everything is reduced down to tokens and symbols. But the game does evoke some theme via pictures and event labels, and also evokes a sense of a quest/journey, a very effective sense of dread when drawing tiles from the bag, and accomplishment when Sauron is beaten.

RATING: 5
UPDATE: As so many more co-op games come out, I have a tough time suggesting this one. It’s pretty foundational in gaming history, but I can’t see suggesting this one over something like one of the many flavors of Pandemic.

UPDATE: There are so many new co-op games out now that build on what Lord of the Rings originally did and does it better. I'm starting to feel like I have more interesting choices in a game like Pandemic or Ghost Stories than in LotR.

This is a hard game for me to rate or to even explain why I like it.

On one level, I understand what all the detractors say -- this takes a great theme and makes it into what sounds like a dry Knizia offering ("What are we doing? Managing a hand of cards?").

HOWEVER, I really feel the tension in this game. Whenever we play, I really have a great time -- maybe it's the art, maybe it's the story, I don't know -- all I know is that usually I feel like we've been put through the ringer and if we lose right away, we usually reset and go at it all over again.

Whether you love it or hate it (and there seems to be no middle ground on this one), you have to admit that Knizia made a very unique game -- a game where everyone is fighting against the game itself and it is very fun because of it.

A big plus for the art.
A big minus for the terrible rulebook.

RATING: 9
A huge thank you to Danny Mack for this gift!

Edition owned: 2nd Hasbro edition (2001) (see differences [thread=904507]here[/thread]).

Best Tolkien themed family board game, is cooperative.  (Compare with: Shadows Over Camelot.)  2-5 players, 90 min, complexity 2/5.  Very good game for gamers, and works well even with two players.  Can be very tense.  Comparison with Shadows Over Camelot: SoC is more accessible/casual/fun/social especially with the traitor element, stronger theme, higher production quality, individual contributions are more important, lighter, less linear than LotR, needs more players, more but simpler decisions (eg I'm at the Grail quest so I play a Grail card); LotR is more gamey (less psychology and more strategic/puzzle-like because you have more resources and more complex decisions), more replay value, more cooperative and less individual play, playable with only two players.

RATING: 9
The first Euro I ever owned - if I'm not mistaken, my parents gave it to me in 2001 or 2002. It's also a fantastic game, though, so the rating isn't purely sentimental.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I have to rate this higher than Pandemic because it offers much better choices.  For a Knizia game, this actually delivered on the theme.  I'm sure the terrific artwork helped my imagination get going as well. This game shines with cooperation. You need to plan together, work together, and sacrifice together in order to get through some really tough spots.  If the point of a coop is to "enforce" teamwork to win, then this game succeeds tremendously. 

Each board is a scenario within the game with a different emphasis and there are choices and consequences for getting through something quickly or not.  Each player has a special power that they use to collect different types of resources that will need to be discarded to help avoid events (which are frighteningly bad) or help exit the scenario board. At the same time, there's a corruption track whereby Sauron and the hobbits move towards each other. If one of the hobbits moves onto the same space as Sauron, game over man!

For my money, I much prefer this coop over the other two old school coops, Pandemic and Ghost Stories, and after having played this not till after both of those, I'm not sure I get why people went gaga for those two when this design offers more interesting choices and variation. The hand of cards you have offers more individual decisions than the other two. From my experience, I would've guessed the order was Ghost Stories > Pandemic > LOTR. 

Of the more modern cooperative games, I prefer the way newer designs give players more individual decision points in the form of cards. The cards of Pandemic and LOTR are simple compared to what players manage in Gears of War and LOTR:TCG, and I prefer those two as my current favorite coops.

RATING: 7
This game continues to be really f'ing hard. And that just adds to the appeal, to be honest. I want to play it partly just because I want to beat the damn thing. 
Not to say it isn't also fun, though, because it is! I think the game mechanics, abstract as they are, actually blend really well with the theme - especially for a Knizia! The whole idea of self-sacrifice, and putting the quest above all else, is obviously central to LotR.

That being said, though, this game really falls into the co-op pitfall of one or two people playing the game for the rest of the group, especially if there's a disparity in experience amongst the group. So that's a bit of a downer.


Update: Finally beat it, and honestly it was anti-climactic. Nobody even died! Strange as it seems, I think I prefer this game when we lose. Must be time to mix in the expansions, to ensure inevitable defeat.

RATING: 6
Played 20+ times with 2-5p.
Ratings history: 10|10|8|8|8........|6.....|8......|6
[b](Rating is much higher when played with an expansion.)[/b]

[b]Lord of the Rings co-op that - when played with a group of people that know each other - seems to play bigger and better than the sum of it's somewhat antiquated mechanics. After more than a decade of enjoyment, I'm finally ready to trade this.[/b]

PROS
- Gorgeous art
- Mostly easy to teach/play
- Socially more interesting than the mechanics would seem to show
- Two boards of pressure (Corruption track and Activity board) are my kind of thing
- short duration
CONS
- Some finicky rules that are easy to miss (who gets the ring; ringbearer gets more cards; etc.)
- co-ops have come a long way: this feels a bit clunky now (but expansions improve it considerably)

Played 20+ times. Played with whole range of players and enjoyed all. Even people who don't know the books usually enjoy the game due to the social & co-operative nature. Fans of the book ought to really appreciate this game. Unique and intriguing. I recommend it. Best when adding in new players. Great social game. Lots of variants to spice things up a bit too. The best thing to do is get the Friends & Foes expansion which makes this game really shine by adding difficulty and tension. And for the ultimate tension addition, get the Sauron expansion, where one player now takes the role of he who shall not be named. [o]

RATING: 8
Our gaming group has more or less "solved" this game. When we have 3-4 players, we can usually win it without breaking a sweat. My wife and I still occasionally enjoy it as a 2-player game, which is difficult no matter what. Even if you know exactly what needs to be done, getting the right combination of cards and tile draws is tough. We've never won the 2-player game, but have come close twice.

None of this has diminished our love for the game, though, which is the best game purchase we've made since Carcassonne.  The tension and the need for real cooperation make it a classic.  

My reasoning for dropping my rating two whole points: I pretty much only want to play the base game now when it's just me and my wife, or when we're introducing new players. With 3-4 experienced players, I like to add [thing=2449]Friends & Foes[/thing] (which remains a solid 10), and hope to give the other expansions a fair shot soon.

RATING: 10
This is the ultimate 'journey' game to me.  By the time you crack open all the scenario boards and pay for each step of progress with painful discards you really feel as though you've been on a long adventure across many lands.  This game is an epic boardgame.  It's not easy to jump right into and I consider the rules complex, mostly due to the way they're written.  Lots of words with bits of info buried inside long paragraphs.  It made the game hard to learn for me.  Still, I enjoy it with the Friends and Foes expansion and consider it a 'one of a kind' must have for my collection.  The game is long, dark, ambitious and beautiful.  Whenever I play it I notice what a great value it is with all the separate boards and the loads of fantastic artwork. Great cards and bits as well.  I enjoy it two player and it scales very well.

Note... This was the first boardgame I'd bought in at least ten years and I picked it up due to the theme.  I was looking for something simple to play with the kids and was disappointed in it's complexity.  The kids watched in horror as the hobbits died one at a time.  I had no idea who this Knizia guy was and what a Eurogame was.  Boy did I learn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is kind of the grandfather of all coop games (I know it's not the first one, but it's the first one I played and I'd like to think that this is the game that made people realize that coop games can actually work and therefore started the recent trend of coop games). I have played it a lot during the years and when I played it quite recently with the Sauron expansion I was amazed at how well this game stood up against many of the newer additions to the genre.

I love the story arc in this game with the tension slowly building up the closer you get to Mordor. Many coop games are purely tactical (and therefore can be perceived as more hectic almost from the first turn), whereas LOTR has strategic elements that reward long-term planning. If you only play with the current turn in mind and don't look ahead, you will most surely lose the game in the end. During the first half of the game you get spammed with resources, and when you leave Lorien you usually have so many cards that you think you'd never run out of them, until you realize you will hardly get ANY more cards during the other half of the game. Therefore every cardplay is kind of agonizing and almost gives you a bad conscience: ("I really should haved saved this card for Mordor!")

I also like the fact that there are at least two different valid overall strategies to each board, and you really have to discuss those strategies with the other players. Of course this game can suffer from the infamous "alpha player syndrom", but I think that has more to do with the people you play with than with the game itself.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A decent coop game and one of the first I've heard. It has some weird mechanisms which I didn't quite enjoy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
When I first read the rules it looked like this was just gonna be a luckfest. Flip over tiles, draw cards, hope you get the right cards to combat the tiles, roll the die...but, there's a lot more to it. Not suprising though since this is a Knizia game. The cooperative nature of the game is very unique and makes this game a lot of fun. Trying to work out a plan with the other players while still trying to keep yourself alive can be difficult. The game gets very tense when Sauron and the Hobbits get close to each other on the corruption line. While I can understand why some people don't like this game, I was pleasantly surprised. 

I had this rated higher, but I'm dropping it to a 7.5. Playing Pandemic has actually made me like LotR less. I don't dislike it at all (it's still a 7.5), but Pandemic is just so much more streamlined, and plays so much quicker.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Lord of the Rings is a super-designed co-op that has more to do with player decision-making than complex gameplay.  While I find the game a bit no-frills, its focus is not supposed to be particularly in-depth; it is a game meant to bring gamers (who are normally opponents) together to defeat Sauron (a classic enemy!).  Thematically it works, if you understand the source material. This can be a very dry experience for those who are not interested in the source material or not a fan of the somewhat abstract gameplay.  But for me I find everything comes together nicely and creates a solid, balanced experience of pitting four (or five) brave Hobbits against the forces of Mordor.

UPDATE: I bought this for a friend's young child (8) for Christmas, so he could "play a big game with Daddy & Uncle Pierce" - we had a fantastic time.. RATING: 7
[b][i]Lord of the Rings[/i][/b] is still one of my favorite cooperative games. The game requires you to properly assess your threat and constantly manage risks versus rewards; missteps are severely punished. This game is difficult, as are all good co-op games.

The game follows the story in the Lord of the Rings trilogy, consequently you're fighting through the same story each game. This could mean that repeated plays end up feeling very similar. Fortunately, the extreme difficulty means that you're constantly trying to improve your game. Also, the random tile draw leads to large variances in ideal strategy which leads to surprisingly dissimilar games.

RATING: 7
Lord of the Rings is enjoyable for two players who are looking for a cooperative experience that is not too simple and not too complex. The turn structure is straightforward, but each turn offers meaningful decisions that can have far-reaching effects. Luck is a heavy factor, but we feel that we still have a chance to win even when luck runs against us. Housekeeping at the end of turns and conflicts is not too burdensome for us. The quality of components in the Silver Line edition are just fine for the price we paid for the game, and the artwork is lovely and evocative. We are happy to keep this game in our collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The theme fits this one so well and there are few other games with so much tension. It really is pretty thrilling watching the tiles flip one by one and seeing how bad things are going to get for you. It actually maintains a nice thematic feel which is pretty rare for a Knizia game. Granted, the differences between fighting and hiding aren’t really present mechanically, but it works because it’s such a birds-eye view of the story. The "working together" aspect is great for playing with new players (as long as you can keep yourself from making decisions for them) and also allows you to play by yourself if you want. This game just feels so streamlined and balanced. Very re-playable, scales beautifully, great components, unique mechanics. You have to respect this game as one of the forerunners of the co-op game movement that has become so popular lately. Especially for fans of the source material, this one can still be fun, but it lacks a lot of the “figuring out the puzzle” that its predecessors have. Given that, that randomness can be a big factor, and most of the decisions involve risk/reward calculations about what type of cards to play first.

RATING: 10

edit: changed the score to 10
This is simply the most thematic game of my whole collection.
I've played the game with various groups of people, and the thing that amazes is that, when people start bickering, the games goes downwards, as if that plastic ring (which could have been so much better) was actually having the power to corrupt.... how awesome is that?  
When people talk of integration with theme, they usually mean a simulation of real-life actions by game systems - here the game thematically reproduces the moods, the feelings and the type of choices the protagonists of the Lord of the Rings live through.  The game also strikes a perfect balance between luck and planning.
This game is the pinacle of thematic integration in games; true it requires some effort, just like good books, but unlike books you can play Lords of the Rings again and again and again, and find it all the more perfect.

RATING: 10
While this was the first Euro-style cooperative game (it isn't the first cooperative game, if you consider the Family Pastimes cooperative games in which cooperation is the goal, as well as the lesson of the game -- in which the game is not always a challenge), it still holds up really well today -- so much that it is still my favorite cooperative game. This game in some ways resists the alpha player mode that occasionally occurs with other cooperative games (i.e., Pandemic) through sheer unpredictability (while, of course, being balanced by someone with a PhD in mathematics). While a bit of reading ahead in terms of events aids in identifying 'easier' routes, that does not mean that they are simple. Either way, there is enough stuff to keep track of (especially if you add in expansions) that players are almost required to keep track of differing areas of the board (and here an alpha becomes a welcomed coordinating captain, a la Space Alert). While the game is incredibly abstracted, the tension and the art evoke an increasingly (long) difficult journey to mount Doom in Mordor.

(In Fredericton)

RATING: 7
[Initial Impression: 7]
This is a very well-themed and compelling cooperative game, making it an excellent choice for LotR fans and for less competitive groups.  The game is quite hard  to complete, which is a big plus for replayability (although it can be made easier by starting Sauron on a higher number), and there are a number of tense decisions to be made on each quest.  You'll be high-fiving each other when the ringbearer reaches Mount Doom after seeing several companions die and then just barely avoids the gaze of Sauron to finally win by the skin of your teeth.  Gameplay can sometimes feel mechanical, and one player who knows the game well can turn it into a multiplayer solitaire by telling others  their "best" option.  In general, it can provide a nice break from the cutthroat games I usually prefer, and is best with a group that can really appreciate and enter into the LotR atmosphere.  Although I generally prefer Shadows over Camelot for a cooperative choice, mainly because of the traitor mechanism and the variety of event cards in that game, I think LotR has an edge for depth of theming and the edge-of-your-seat feeling.

RATING: 8.5
Thematically, the game aims to be a condensed version that retells the legendary story of our hobbits, and it does so very well. It encapsulates the story almost perfectly, until you see Aragorn as a card you burn in one turn and, well, he's gone for good. 9/10 for thematics.

The game part of the game works well. It is simple to explain, yet it has depth to it. A low barrier of entry is appreciated, so I believe the game successfully achieves what it sets out to do: A one hour speed run through Minas to Mordor.

What's keeping it from being a 10 is that there's nothing really special to it.

RATING: 5
It's a Knizia, but doesn't feel anything like his other games. This game immerses the players in J.R.R.Tolkien's world, and follows the books/movies faithfully. The game starts out slow, but rather quickly becomes a desperate struggle for survival, let alone trying the destroy the ring. The abstract mechanics are simple, and interesting, as they do not dumb-down the game, but allow the players to make crucial life-or-death choices almost continually. All players are engaged, as each represents one of the five hobbits in the Fellowship, and each has a special power that can be used to break one of the rules. Using all five powers is essential to winning the game, and since resources are extremely limited, players must choose on every turn (not just their turn), to surrender resources (hobbit cards, life tokens, shields) or get corrupted, for the good of the Fellowship. Sauron approaches menacingly from one end of the Corruption Track, while the hobbits start uncorrupted at the other end. The game ends if the ring-bearer hobbit is corrupted to the point it meets Sauron OR the ring-bearer gets to Mt. Doom and destroys the ring. The corruption track is pure genius, as it conveys a sense of urgency, and impending doom. The tension is thick, and the action is fast. The game goes rather quickly at about 90- minutes, and is fun whether the hobbits win or lose. I personally think the game is too simplistic/abstract, but the feelings it generates for all who play it is unmistakable. This is a cooperative game, similar to Shadows Over Camelot, and Arkham Horror. It really depends on what people are familiar with. If they've read LTR or seen the movies, this should be a hit at the table. I will need to get the F&F and/or the Sauron expansion, to see if it can up the complexity for my tastes.

Update (01/20/12): Initial rating was a '7'. It's been a year since the last play, and my disdain for cooperative games has solidified. I'm open to breaking this out and teaching it to some newbie who is an LOTR fan, but have no desire to play it with my current group. I think it is time to start finding this a new home. It's a '5'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Great cooperative game!! One of my family's favorite.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]ONE GAME TO RULE THEM ALL, ONE GAME TO FIND THEM; ONE GAME TO BRING THEM ALL AND AT THE TABLE SEAT THEM DOWN TILL WE WIN THIS DAMN THING[/b]

This is the oldest game I own. While I learned how to play games with my sister’s Monopoly copy  twelve years ago, I learned about [i]board gaming[/i] after I got this game for Christmas in 2000. After a few years of not actually being able to play this game since many pieces of it were lost, I finally found replacements when I subscribed in BGG. Since then, this game has seen all the action it didn’t see in over five years. And just like it was when I started playing it, is is still one of the hardest and most daring games I have ever seen.

In this game, the players take the roles of Hobbits (Frodo, Sam, Pippin, Marry and Fatty Bolger, added to this game just so it could be played with 5 players), each with his own special ability and miniature of matching color. The game consists of a series of scenarios, where players gather strength, spend cards and face the adversities of Moria, Helm’s Deep and other holiday spots of the like. The game ends (If possible, I might add) when the ring is destroyed in the depths of Mount Doom.

The game turn, though hard and filled with dangers for the players (If not, for the active player at least) is a very simple step of the game. And so are the various steps and phases within the game. The rules are very simple and not much questioning is involved during game play, except for the obvious exceptions regarding events and cards being played.

As a Knizia game, The Lord of the Rings comes as a fresh breath of air from all the abstractness of his previous releases and projects (Not that I’m complaining about them…) and lack of a real permanent, needed theme for a game. Unlike the other games of Herr Reiner, TLOTR has a lot more of game depth developed for the sole purpose of making this game a particular choice within his vast collection of designs. It does not behave or play out abstractly and the theme is very well mix and put together around the game mechanics and art, and viceversa (John Howe really topped himself with this game’s artwork).

The level of difficulty on this game is astounding, and incredibly as it may seem, the people I’ve taught how to play this can’t get enough of it, not mattering if they win, lose or draw (It’s just a manner of speech, you can’t draw in this game, and if you do, you’ve lost). I think that happens because the game itself is not only a real challenge and a source of infinite strategy, but also because people don’t like games being smarter than them or better than them in any way. TLOTR therefore has become a sort of competition within my regular gaming group. I don’t think it will ever not be.

[b][i]I. Criteria[/b][/i]

Mechanics: :d10-9:
Design & Art:  :d10-1::d10-0:
Packaging & Presentation: :d10-1::d10-0:
Theme: :d10-1::d10-0:
BGG Factor: :d10-7:

[b][i]II. Sliders[/i][/b]

Abstract [b]________[COLOR=#FF0000][9][/COLOR]_[/b] Themed: As mentioned above, this is the one Knizia Game that relies heavily on the theme, and I don’t see the mechanics of this game being used properly or to the full extent as they are in this game, on another one.  Though the basic ideas of the turn flow and phases  are common place for many games, TLOTR actually puts them all together in such a way that trying to overlook or ignore them is not far from impossible. No wonder the ways players have to “prevent” certain things from happening are found in the game elements themselves.
 
Simple [b]______[COLOR=#FF0000][7][/COLOR]___[/b] Complex: As with games with so much bits and symbols, the game may be seen as complex by outsiders and they wouldn’t be wrong if they tried the game. The complexity of it relies on the heavy amount of symbols a player has to know every time and how they appear and act on the game itself. However, the game play itself is not too complex, except for the fact that a lot of decision making must be made before each turn.

Strategic [b]________[COLOR=#FF0000][9][/COLOR]_[/b] Tactical: Long-term strategies don’t work well in this game, because of the high risk element involved in each “draw” step of the turn. Event tiles can change everything or the can change very little, so tactics are a better choice. However, minor strategic action plan may be proposed at the beginning of the game, as for certain cards the players use and when would the precise moment would be to use them. 
 
Luck [b]_[COLOR=#FF0000][2][/COLOR]________[/b] Skill: Considering the fact that in almost 12 years of me owning this game, I have only won with other players around 8 times. It’s safe to say that luck plays a major part in this game. If you leave too much to chance and don’t prepare yourself, you might end up dead sooner than expected.

Fiddly [b]___[COLOR=#FF0000][4][/COLOR]______[/b] Elegant: The rule book is not very well written, and it lacks a better flow of explanations and examples. Though the rule book itself is a bit flawed and unclear, once the game has been played a few times with the rule book open, it could eventually see less and less light, since the only things important enough to ask about the rules are usually known by other players.
 
Indirect interaction [b]_________[COLOR=#FF0000][10][/COLOR][/b] Direct interaction: Despite the fact that dying alone in this game is possible and very probable, team-work is almost always a force behind the players actions. Trying to stay alive as a group won’t work unless everyone’s ready to sacrifice everything to destroy the Ring, no matter who’s the Ring-bearer in that moment. And still, survival of everyone in the game is not guaranteed. 

Cutthroat [b]__________[/b] Friendly: Playing solo, trying to be a hero, thinking glory is for the strong and not for the group will most definitely land you harsh treatment and maybe, even the scorn of the other players. It is a co-op game, so thinking co-operatively is important, at least in the sense of considering the other player’s decisions before doing something risky..

Heavy use of language [b]__[COLOR=#FF0000][3][/COLOR]_______[/b] No language needed: The whole game relies heavily on symbols and combinations of symbols, so there’s not much language involved In the game, except for the event track on the side of each scenario and the little text-boxes on the main board. Those are usually simple to understand and as usual, some of the players will know what happens in each one of them.

[b][i]III. Number of players recommended[/i][/b]: 5.

[b]CHEERS![/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First played May 30th, 2008. Taught in 20min, played in 1hr with 3 players. This is a competitive co-opretive quest mission game. To win, you must throw the ring into the fire, otherwise you lose. Each player is given a starting hand of cards which you must use wisely. You go on mini adventures (Moria, Helms Deep, Shelobs Lair, Mordor) using these cards and are not easily replenished. Froto is the start player and bears the ring to start. On your turn you play cards to capture shields and 1 of each of three life points(ring, heart, and sun). For each life points you don't capture during each quest you lose one life point and move toward Sauron on the life track. All hobbits (Froto, Sam, Pippin, Merry, and ??) start on zero and Sauron starts on 12. First you turn over tiles which may have events that do(or play cards to skip), or have an icon of one of the sub-quests(move that sub-quest token forward). Keep drawing tiles until you get a non-event tile. Next you play two cards, each hobbit has a special ability(Pippin can play any two cards; Froto can play white cards as wild; etc). Collect the icon on the track that you move to and through. The Ring Bearer can place the ring on and pass over spaces without effect. Once you reach the end of the main quest path, you get a blind shield(values 1-3). 5 Sheilds can enlist a spell from Gandoff once for each of the 6 spells. There are many times when you must roll the die. Sides are blank(best), 1,2,3pix (which you move your hobbit closer to Sauron), 2 cards(lose two), and Sauron eye(move Sauron closer to each of you). If you can't fufil any request that player is out of the game and if it's the ring bearer, everyone loses.

RATING: 7
Finally played this one right and it got a ton easier (although, I'm not terribly sure that's a good thing).  For anyone struggling to stay alive at 12, remember the ring bearer draws two cards at the beginning of every scenario board and that event tiles with three icons mean one OR the other, not both.

Even though a normal game is a little over/under an hour, the journey still seems epic and grueling like it should and so I must commend the game for that.  Man, the tension in Mordor is intense...

Update:  I've played this with some other people who I came to find really disliked it.  I think you have to have a pretty strong tie to the theme to enjoy it given that, in truth, the game is merely a supped up version of Go Fish with events and die rolls.

RATING: 6.9
Oct08: Well, I got to try this one after Pandemic, but I think it still lived up to my expectations.  I enjoyed the theme and even enjoyed the play style.  My wife really enjoyed the personal goals that seem to be missing from the later coops.  In LotR, you have to care some about your own hobbit.  It's true that the group wins if the ring gets dunked, but it's real hard to just let Sauron eat your hobbit if you can help it.

There seem to be plenty of expansions that I don't really need to try.  Maybe if I played the crap out of the base game.  The tile flip of doom definitely does add to the tension.  As does the severe lack of resources.

If you at all are interested in coops or LotR, I'd recommend to give this a go.  It's an enjoyable game that I had a lot of fun with.

RATING: 7
Fun game with a great theme fairly true to story. Some difficult choices and original mechanics with the co-op play make it nice to have also some turns are quite tension filled. Update: It really is a little too much like solitaire for my liking and someone else calling this "go fish" isn't really as far off as it sounds. That said, it is still a unique co-op experience in one of the greatest themes ever (IMHO of course) so it stays in my collection for now with an occasional play. The componets and theme definitely help keep the rating where it is.

Update: Down from 7.5 => 7 for reasons above and too many other good games to choose from so not being suggested.

Update: Well well, before playing so many games it seems I dismissed this as mostly luck and a solitaire type game. While this IS true to some degree it is really a brilliant and very unique design overall and I have really come to appreciate it now after some more plays many years later. Lots of planning with timing especially and resources re the cards. It is tense, very thematic, unique and fun which makes it quite enjoyable to play and it scales down to two players which is probably my preferred way to play. Don't dismiss this too soon and trade it like I almost did unless of course you don't enjoy the theme.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Reasonably challenging coop game.  Although some folks claim it to be very difficult to win, I don't find it so.  As long as you follow certain guidelines like making sure to see what the next Event will be and being prepared for it, it tends to go fairly easily.  You are playing members of the Fellowship of the Ring and you play cards to move the expedition along.  You also have the Ring to help out once per area.  You have to survive through four areas.  Each turn you flip over a tile, which is often bad.  If it is bad, you then have to flip over another tile, which can lead to cascading doom.  There is always a nice tension and sense of imminent doom, recreating the desperate atmosphere of the books.  Each player has a hidden hand of cards and so you have to talk about strategies together, which is good.  Particularly enjoyable if you are an LOTR fan.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I love playing this game with friends and family.  The decisions are tense and agonizing.  Not a game I could play everyday but when I do play it this is tons of fun.  I can't understand the criticism that there aren't very many interesting choices.  This game has TONS of choices that are interesting because they all seem like LIFE or DEATH choices.  The choices aren't in where you are going to go just like the novel there was only one place in which they could go to destroy the ring.  The choices are in how are you going to survive the journey there.  There are tons of resources that you have to decide on when to use and when to save.  Which track should you advance on, when should you save your shields.  There are tons of different ways to fend of impending disaster.  I think a lot of the people who don't think there are many choices didn't have the interest to learn all of the interesting tricks you can do to survive the ordeal.  That's what I love about it is that it seems like an ordeal that you have to survive and your choices DO make a difference.  After playing this after a long break I realized why I love this game in the first place.

RATING: 4
2012-09-25

Initial Rating: 6.0 (May 2011)

One the first games I ever bought and I really REALLY wanted to like it as I’m a huge LOTR fan, also when I bought it I was sure all Knizia games were instant classics. Sadly this game (and other Knizia titles I have played) lead me to a rude awakening. It took me more than a year to play this again…. There is just no interest in me or my game groups. 

I like cooperative games so that’s not why I dislike this game. Somehow I expected a game from Fantasy Flight and Lords of the Rings to be hugely thematic, however when I play this I don´t feel the theme anywhere, it´s more an abstract game. I find the game has a lot of luck in the events tiles, you could be wiped out with the very first event so, for me, the success of the game is determined by the luck of this tiles. Also, it seems there is not a meaningful decision in the game, either you have the needed card or you don’t. Not much more choice than that. The game moves on its own that’s to the (hated) event tiles so I don’t feel I’m playing anything.

On the good side the components and art are very well done, the Sauron piece is solid and feel nice to the touch. Also this is the game that knocked out Knizia as an autobuy designer from me (Relationship Tighrope also helped)

I suspect the game could be better if I find the right crow for this, I will give it one more chance before I get rid of it, but right now I’m very disappointed in the game.

It´s a shame I don’t like this game, but there are a lot of better coop games out there (Arkham Horror and Space Alert come to mind).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If there's a phrase that inspires terror in my heart, it is "Reiner Knizia's Lord of the Rings." But you know what? This is a solid cooperative game, and it is the closest Herr Doktor comes to a living, breathing theme. It's still like entering the Knizia echo chamber, in which Frodo, Sam, Gandalf and company faintly call out from the clockwork depths of hand-management, resource management (yep) and generic, catch-all story choices. But if you listen, you can hear them.

In 2009, LOTR occupies a good middle ground for pure co-op games: longer than most recent titles, but a good deal shorter than Arkham Horror. The pacing matches Pandemic, but the game runs twice as long, broken up into a handful of tightly packed scenarios: Moria, Helm's Deep, Shelob's Lair, and Mordor. Each scenario thrives off of the same tension: let's get the heck out of here (Giant Spider, run!), or let's strengthen the bonds of the fellowship. Nothing stops the group from blitzing Moria with un-hobbit-like abandon, avoiding all of the plot-crises that may arise. But a power-mad rush plays to the ring, leading the pure of heart deeper into Sauron's corruption.

Play is simple. On your turn you draw a tile from a bag, which may trigger a story-threat to the fellowship. You keep drawing until a harmless tile comes up, and then make your move. There's some ability to play to the odds here, but unlike the controlled epidemic-distribution in Pandemic, nothing prevents drawing every bad tile in a row. I've seen this happen once in five games, and sure enough, we lost that game, though Sam and Pippen were near the summit of Mt. Doom at Sauron's moment of ultimate triumph. The possibility for such total disaster feels a bit out of place, but we certainly got a cliffhanger out of it. The scenarios themselves differ in their priorities, but I worry that the same set of goals will hurt the game's long-term replayability. It seems to me that you're encouraged to take your time (to the extent the tiles allow you) in Moria and Helm's deep, and to rush Shelob's lair and Mordor with all the saved-up ferocity you can muster. At least, that's won it for us 4/5 times. Overall though, this is a must-try for co-op fans, outshining every peer except Pandemic and Space Alert.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a really nice co-op game in which everyone feels like they can contribute. We played with the friends and foes expansion and defeated the foes. Cards are played to progress along either the main path or two side paths for one of several boards. All give mostly good tokens, but sometimes the die has to be rolled which usually brings you and Sauron closer together. There are also escalating challenges for each board, so you need to progress on the main path and get to the next board before being overwhelmed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very tight resource management co-op/solo game. The game plays out over several boards depicting the events in the Lord of the Rings. There are several tracks on each board and players must play cards to advance on these tracks collecting resources based on the spaces they land on. At the same time random draws are causing an event track to advance which generally causes bad things to happen.

Players have to balance immediate benefits, long-term goals and collectively optimize to ensure they reach Mt. Doom before the eye of Sauron corrupts them.

Since you progress through the same boards every game I am a bit worried about replay. However for $30 I think I'll definitely get my money's worth. The game easily has 15-20 plays before it gets too repetitive... and at that point there are expansions to consider.

RATING: 6
I appreciate the innovative aspect of this game - if you look at the context in which this game was released there was really nothing else like it.  However, at this point in time, with so many different cooperative games around, many of them playing in a shorter amount of time, it doesn't really distinguish itself enough to warrant repeat play.

I should note that it is amazing how thematic the game feels in spite of such abstract game mechanics.  You're just moving pieces down the board, picking up cards and discarding them, but somehow you do feel the tension of getting closer to your destination, but victory slipping through your fingers as you approach.  Knizia is definitely shortchanged when it comes to thematic integration, how he does it is really impressive.

RATING: 7.5
One of my favorite co-ops.  For some reason, this one doesn't get the same attention that other co-op style games (Pandemic, BSG, Shadows over Camelot) do, which is too bad as it's one of the best marriages of theme and gameplay in the genre.  Maybe the Knizia factor hurts it, but, really, with or without expansions, this one has brutal tension up to the end.  Yes, the scoring and symbols definitely bear the mark of their creator, but I actually find this system much more tense and interesting than, say, the cards in BSG or Shadows.

Only drawback is that the expansions become necessary in order to avoid staleness.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Megpróbáltam átgondolni, hogy mi miatt nem tudtam megszeretni ezt a társast.
Egyrészt a szabálykönyv szerkesztése, megfogalmazása rengeteget rontott a kezdeti lelkesedésemen.
Másrészt úgy érzem, hogy egyszerűen túl nagy a szerencse szerepe a játékban. Már írtam párszor, nincs bajom a játékokat befolyásoló véletlenekkel, de itt egyszerűen alapjaiban határozza meg az egész eredményességet, és ha szerencsétlenül fordítgatjuk az eseménylapokat, akkor aztán a legfantasztikusabb játékkal is esélyes, hogy akár már a második kaland környékén is elkaphat minket Szauron.
Furcsa az is, hogy nem mutathatjuk meg egymásnak a lapokat, de beszélhetünk róluk, amennyit csak akarunk. Tehát elmesélhetem, hogy mi van nálam, de nem mutathatom meg. Ez nekem nem logikus.
A játékidőt 60 perc környékére írják. A második próbálkozásunk során már gyorsan, gördülékenyen mentek a dolgok, de így is több, mint 90 perc kellett ahhoz, hogy meghaljunk. 
De talán a legnagyobb bajom a lineáris játékmenet. Mindig ugyanazon a pontokon kell végigmenni, és tulajdonképpen az újabb játékokban a saját korábbi eredményünkkel tudunk versenyezni. Olyan, mint egy számítógépes játék, aminek van egy pályája, amit teljesíteni kell. Persze ott is lehet állítani nehézségi fokozatot, de nekem attól nem lesz újszerű az élmény. Értem én, hogy ha hűek akarunk lenni az eredeti cselekményhez, akkor ezt így lehet megoldani, és ebből csak annyi a tanulság, hogy az ilyen jellegű társasjátékok nem nekem valók.

Ha egy társaságban előkerülne A Gyűrűk Ura, nem menekülnék sikítva, és persze, leülnék játszani, de az az igazság, hogy magamtól nem venném elő.

RATING: 6.5
Trying to simulate the journey of the Hobbits leads to quite some railroading, but which offers opportunities and necessity to plan ahead. Unfortunately the cooperative nature is somewhat limited. It might have been better if it were possible to exchange cards more often (similar to what Castle Panic does).
The biggest negative factor seem to be the event tiles. On one hand it feels unintuitive to draw tiles until you get a non-event one; on the other hand you can be screwed pretty quickly when you happen to draw event after event.
In its heyday it probably was revolutionary, but nowadays it seems to have been surpassed by most other cooperative games.
{CHMM}

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is the only co-op game I own, and being a fan of LOTR I thought it would be more fun, but unfortunately it falls a bit short.  Thematically it is fairly strong and the board art it excellent.  The cards and bits are also of above average quality.  I found that the mechanics connected well to the theme as the hobbits fall deeper into darkness as they approach Mordor.  The game fails to address the fact that the hobbits were separated at the end of Fellowship and it is possible for Pippin, Merry, or even Fatty to travel to Mount Doom and destroy the ring.  As a co-op game this one falls into an odd trap when playing with a mix of gamers and non-gamers.  The gamers will tend to map out all the best options before non-gamers really get their bearing and the non-gamers end up being ordered around and often feel like seat warmer.  After just a few plays it is easy to solve the game every time.  Despite my love of all things LOTR, this game will probably collect a lot of dust at my house.  I have not tried any expansions and it is possible that they add more depth to the game.  It was fun for a few plays, despite the fact that I felt like I was playing solitaire with four warm bodies.

RATING: 3
Game? Well, that's a loose translation of the word. It felt like I was playing with a three year old that kept taking the toys away from me saying "MINE!". Great art and actually a decent concept but it tries so hard to be true to the story that it takes all decisions out of your hand. Played it enough times that I figured out the exact timing of when to play cards and never could lose after that. Then played it as a group with all the expansions and realized that it was more an excercise in telling other players what to play and when. Those that were new just did what the others said and those that knew the game just excercised their previous knowledge. It's boring. It's boring. It's boring. If you like having no excercise in decision making and relish just watching something happen in front of you that you have no control over, go watch the movies.

Now, I must admit, when I first bought it, all the neat pieces (especially the eye of Sauron) and the artwork really sucked me in. The fact that I could play solo also appealed to me (don't get much time with other people), I played it through about 5 or 6 times, beat it and then continued to beat it every time after that. About six months later I played with a group of people and this time the expansions were included. I found myself telling everybody what they needed to do (and I'm NOT a bossy person) in order to win. They did. We did. Game over. Everyone just sat there looking at the board in wonder that it was that boring. I give it a slightly higher rating than a 1 only because it did entertain for a short time and the bits are very nice, but I really cannot be lead to believe it's worth spending a massive amount of money or time on ever again.

RATING: 7
I think the best thing about this game is the sense of urgency it instills - something I haven't quite got from any other game. There are constant sacrifices of things that you'd like to do but can't risk taking the time and allowing Sauron to draw closer. Very nice artwork on the boards as well.

Some luck involved in drawing the tiles - a run of events tiles drawn at the beginning of a board can end the game. 

One problem is I think we incorporated the expansions too soon - we were not yet able to beat the basic game and added in the Friends & Foes and then the Sauron expansion which both seem to add more difficulty. We've recently removed the expansions and gone back to the regular game so we're able to *almost* win again.
