RATING: 8
I am more susceptible to flights of fancy than some of my colleagues so the theme is ok for me.  However, the themes real strength is to provide an efficient framework for the game which is familiar to millions of people.  This familiarity enables a high level of accessibility, and for someone who finds it difficult to explain rules, a narrative tool to aid explanation and understanding of the game.  Without the theme what is left is a beautiful and robust system which challenges and involes each player.  While I feel it lacks the high-levels of tension of Pandemic (the current darling of the co-operative games genre) its accesibility means that each player grasps the game quicker and can contribute earlier therefore avoiding the loudest voice dominating which affects many co-operative games.  A wonderful game which I would enjoy playing in groups of 2-4.  I will never play with a fifth player as I am sure I would get the Fatty card.

RATING: 4
Has the variety and imagination of a co-operative solitare. You can make it difficult, sure, but you can't make it challenging. You can make the game more difficult either by limiting the number of steps that end the game of by ignoring the obvious strategy in order to "experience the story through the game". These, however, don't help at all. The first option is like playing the same co-op solitare as before, but with fewer piles of cards. The latter, in turn, is just imagining some restrictive rules of your own and trying to beat the game in spite of them. Either way, to me, it doesn't really fit the description of a game as it not a form of amusement.

RATING: 7.2

If you play it a lot and get frustrated with this, you may end up tweaking it to make winning a little likelier.  Some parents will really like playing this game with their children because it rewards cooperation.  You can play it with younger children than most other games, because as your child's team-mate, you can help her handle difficult bits.

RATING: 7
A run cooperative game that was of the first in the genre.  Players take on the roll of one of the Hobbits from LotR's.  You are trying to make it to Mordor and discard the Ring in mount doom.  You play cards and work on multiple paths.  Events come out, cause the Eye to move on a track toward the hobbits or possibly to have bad events occur.  

If you make it to Mount Doom and discard the ring you win and there are a number of ways to lose.  

I really like this game.  It is very fun and great for families.

RATING: 6
The card and board art are very nice, which just makes the cheap tokens seem that much cheaper.  The game flows well, following the story line but keeping it simple, and the mechanics mesh well with the theme.  Double-sided boards maintain flavor without it getting unwieldy.  I like cooperative games but hate the way they often end up being one-player directed; this one seems to resist that tendency, which is refreshing.  There's a lot of luck in the tile-drawing; the game can kill you before you even have a chance to play.  There are some workarounds (buy a Gandalf power to reorder the next few tiles, ignore the effects of one tile), but unless you've done well enough to stash away a bunch of extra points, you can't buy those effects.  So it's not on my all-time faves list just because you are pretty much at the game's mercy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Your experience with this game will definitely depend on your gaming group. The first two groups I played with it seemed really dry.  The third group I tried it with, we had a good time with some joking around about the story.  I still prefer other cooperative games (e.g., Shadows over Camelot), but I think LotR has earned itself a place in the rotation.

With more plays, I find this game being enjoyed and requested more than I initially would have thought.  I still usually turn to Pandemic (especially now with the expansion), but this definitely has earned a rotation spot.

RATING: 8
Lots of stuff, but relatively few rules -- and a co-op experience that's unlike any other co-op game I've played. I love that it emulates the feel -- and events -- of the series so well, what with sacrificing people, the steady march towards corruption, and the themes of the different tracks and regions. Plus, it's got beautiful components (revamped Silver Line version is great).

Of the Knizia games I've played, this is the one with the tightest connection to, and strongest expression of, its theme. An all-around awesome game.

---

One of the most original and inventive games I have seen in a decade. The "Magic Flute" of 1990s game design. The way the theme reveals itself in very subtle (and chromeless) ways through player behavior and interaction is stunning. For my money this is Knizia's best design since Modern Art. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've seen so many people rail on this game because it's basically just an abstract game with some LOTR pictures pasted on top. I really enjoy this game, and think it evokes a LOTR feel, but not in the way you would think. When most games try and go for a specific theme, they will have you physically do things related to that theme. A LOTR game might have you move your pieces around and fight orcs. But that's not how this game evokes theme. This game evokes the LOTR theme by making the optimal play to win the game be by sacrificing yourself for another player (most of the time). This game is extremely hard to win, and when you do win, you normally have to win by having one or more players sacrificing themselves, so another player can "carry the ring" to Mount Doom to win the game. In that sense, I think this game evokes the LOTR theme beautifully, because it makes you do LOTR themed things (sacrifice for another) without telling you specifically to do those things. That's pretty cool in my book.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Game in a Nutshell: Cooperative game where players take on the roles of the hobbits taking the ring to Mordor.

Played as: 3 player

Played with: Only the base game.

Pros: Presents a good challenge that ramps up as you move along. I like the progression from one board to the next, creating a sense of progression towards mount doom. The amount of flavor text (very little) seems appropriate, with nothing being bogged down by it. Nice artwork.

Cons: I'm really tiring of Fantasy Flight rulebooks. The game really abstracts things to a point that it's hard to remember there's a larger picture. Going on about "needing a sun token" is not the same as actually feeling like things are grim and the psyches of the hobbits are falling apart.

Other: I played this long after newer more popular co-ops came along and it's hard to point out anything this game does better than those at this point in time.

Best Use: Lord of the Ring junkies have their family game.

RATING: 8.5
This cooperative game is a masterpiece.  In particular, it captures the spirit of the novels much better than the dozens of war/battle games based on the books.  Most of the story is about agonizing choices and being nearly overwhelmed by events.  

It also has good replay value.  Style of play is affected by the size of the party.  The expansions ramp up the difficulty for those who master the base game (I'm getting there.)  Two-player is the hardest, I think.

RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]

[u]02-Aug-2015[/u]:  This is an early cooperative game themed around the Lord of the Rings (obviously).  You must take a number of Hobbits through four conflict boards to reach Mt Doom.  There, you must successfully destroy the Ring before Sauron claims it and brings horror to the lands of Middle Earth.

The game is essentially a card/resource management game.  Each Hobbit has a hand of cards which allows the entire Fellowship to move along their journey.  The card represent various aspects of survival for the Hobbits, including combat, hiding, friendship, and travel.  Each Hobbit also gains up to three attributes on each board, otherwise, they will move closer to Sauron on the corruption track.

If at least one Hobbit makes to Mt Doom and successfully throws in the Ring, the Fellowship wins.

There are lots of things I enjoy about this game.  The cardplay is fantastic.  I really enjoy managing the cards for each of the Hobbits, when to spend them, which ones to spend, and who acquires new ones.  Combine this with the "resources" of those attributes, and you have a really nice hand management game.

I also love that there is player elimination in this game.  As long as one Hobbit can make it to the end, you have a chance to defeat evil.  There is a corruption track which shows how close Sauron is to gaining the ring, as well as how much under the Ring's spell the Hobbits have succumbed.  If a Hobbit and Sauron meet on this track, the Hobbit is out of the game, as he has gone too far into his obsession with the Ring.

As these two forces come closer and closer on the corruption track, the tension of the game increases.  Then, when one, and then another, Hobbit are removed from the game, that tension just skyrockets.

For me, this is also where the theme comes through...that feeling of tension and desperation as the endgame comes nearer.  I enjoy the LotR IP, but I am not a fanboy.  I don't know all the ins and outs of the lore, and I think I miss out on the story while playing the game because of this.  Again, the tension is there.  The "feel" of LotR is there for me but not the narrative.  But that's just me.

Overall, an excellent cooperative game that most gamers should give a look.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
I initially thought, "How bad can it be?" and picked this up to test how much of a sucker I am for the Lord of the Rings theme.

The first game was quite dull, but I did enjoy the theme. We also got demolished, which piqued my interest in trying again. Two tries later, we won and have minimal interest in playing again. (We subsequently realized we had one rule wrong and still feel done with the game.)

There are some minimally interesting decisions on how to handle each scenario and when to expend your resources through out the game. But mostly it just feels like you're being taken for a ride on the random tile flips. Lots of event tiles early on = you're screwed. Not so many = cake walk.

UPDATE: My faith in Knizia has me thinking I "could be talked into" this again, especially with the Sauron expansion. Upped my rating a notch.

The game may not have Pandemic's elegant mechanism for increasing the tension when epidemics spread but fans of Lord of the Rings may find Knizia's contribution to the cooperative games quite immersive.

RATING: 3
I don't get all the positive comments about this game.  I found it to be thoroughly mechanical and boring.  It's all about whether you've got the right cards, and either you've got 'em or you don't.  If you don't, eventually you lose.  Further, when it is time to play cards it's pretty obvious what the best play is.  Yes, I like the manner in which Sauron gets closer and closer.  That's a neat design trick that attempts to capture a sense of increasing tension...it's just that there's no tension.  Like I say, the game boils down to either you've got the cards or you don't.  Play 'em.  Rinse and repeat.  

As someone who re-read Lord of the Rings 6 months ago, I found this game to capture very little that is in the story.  Well, except for the art.  The art is great, and the physical production is top notch.  But the game simply isn't my cup of tea.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fun, unique cooperative game by Knizia where everyone plays a hobbit trying to throw the ring into Mount Doom before Sauron corrupts everyone. Everyone gets involved in the discussion on how the party should plan it's next move. Some people don't like it since 1) there may not be any control if too many event tiles show up 2) it can be viewed as a multiplayer solitarire game, and 3) some people can dominate the decisions of the party. Despite these minor drawbacks, a good solid game.

RATING: 6
Great mechanics. Always love the theme even though it feels slightly painted on to mechanics--but only slightly. Well made came with lots of decision making and tension; not the best ever but always a rewarding couple of hours.

Rating is 8 or higher when the expansions, at least the Friends and Foes one are included.

UPDATE: still a great game but shows its age and its Knizia ishness. You do get the FEEL of having to struggle to move forward so there's a similar feel as LOTR, but it's all done in such an overly abstract way. There are just better coops out now and the latest expansions is like doing a flowchart in the most non thematic way possible. This game is fun for what it is and I enjoy playing it but with the selection of coop games, even Euroesque coop games out there, I'd rather play those than LOTR. Also War of the Ring doesn't take that much longer once you learn rules and is a MUCH better MUCH more thamatic game. Yes it's not euro and its competative but still for a LOTR experience, I'll just play LOTR. This game is good for what it is, but there are better coop options, better LOTR options, and just plain better games that I'd rather play even though this one is enjoyable enough.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The 1st 10-12 minutes of Lord Of The Rings don't involve any actual play. Everyone just stares at the Frodo player as he/she goes through a bunch of scripted actions. Comments like "What's going on?", "Okay, what are we doing?", and "That was the first/second/third/fourth/fifth/sixth turn?!" abound. It is not gameplay,  just extended setup. 

The cooperative aspects of LotR come into play when players are versed in the games mechanics and know what threats they will be facing and how best to approach them.

I think if this game didn't have such lovely artwork it wouldn't be remembered as fondly as it has been 10+ years since its release and if it didn't have the Lord Of The Rings license it wouldn't be remembered at all. Why would anyone consider this a good game? How a game this bad kickstarted the cooperative gaming genre I will never understand.

As a final note, if you actually like this game and want a copy yourself, do not buy the Fantasy Flight Games Silver Line Edition! It is the bargain model. Plastic figures and the metal One Ring have been replaced with cardboard. Terrible! Shame on you Fantasy Flight!

RATING: 8
The components of this game are a mixed bag.  The artwork on the scenario boards is beautiful, as is that on some of the cards.  The rest is just okay.  The plastic, primary-colored Hobbit figures are pathetic.  The rule book is long, but I created a 2 page summary to increase the likelihood of playing again at a later date.  As for play itself, I was not disappointed.  I like cooperative games, and it's particularly fitting that a game about the Fellowship of the Ring should be cooperative.  Working together to overcome obstacles and avoid Sauron lends a certain RPG feel to the game, especially when played with a group of avid LoTR fans, the movie soundtrack playing in the background.  There is a genuine tension and uncertainty in the game that renders a successful completion a real triumph. (4+4)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
While this game does have a moderate dose of luck, great cooperative strategy can overcome bad luck. The game is pretty easy to learn, so you aren't stuck rifling through rule books 3+ plays in. I learned the game fully in one playthrough. That said, it still has enough clever mechanics to keep board game "nerds" interested for at least a handful of plays.  The game is very cooperative, often relying on sacrifice and communication and strategy on actions. The art is fantastic. It's very "old school" and I prefer it to any movie licensed art. If you use a little imagination, you can really get into the theme, especially if you're a LotR fan. I really enjoy this game, and see it as an important part of my cooperative board game collection. It has a few flaws. (Note: Im playing the Fantasy Flight Silver Edition) Namely the Adventure Tiles that you flip during the dungeons, which are called "conflicts" I believe. They're a large stack of very small tiles, and the pile topples easily. You also have to "shuffle" them, which is a pain, as they're unwieldy. Cards would have been MUCH better than tiles in that situation. Im considering making my own cards, even. It's that frustrating. The rule book isn't very concise in it's layout, either. Otherwise, I consider this a great addition to my collection. If you like cooperative adventure games with a moderate dose of luck and strategy, and you like LotR, I think you'll really enjoy it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
"One of the earliest modern pure co-ops, but holds up very well. Thematic and abstract at the same time."

What I like:
- Feels epic (usually in 75-90 minutes)
- Four scenario boards are great
- Unusual mechanisms (moving shared pawns)
- Great components in Hasbro edition

What I don't like:
- Tile draws can be swingy
- Rules are pretty poorly organized
- Player elimination can feel harsh

This is an excellent cooperative game, despite being one of the first modern co-ops. It's a race against time, not a battle (which is fine). It is quite abstract in some ways, but thematically you do feel like little hobbits participating in this quest to save the world.

The weight rating on BGG seems too high, as this seems like a simpler game than others rated around 2.6. I think I would give it a 2.4.

Theme Integration:....:star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:[/c]

Playtime: ~30-60 minutes, maybe a bit longer with 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm on a theme/rpg kick these days, and after reading so many good reviews for this game I had to buy it.  Generally, I like the scripted aspect to it, because really, you're playing the stories through.  The main difference is that in the stories everyone wins.  In this game, its very easy to lose.  If dumb luck doesn't get you, dumb choices will.

When do I play this?:
This is the kind of game that gets played on request.  It's appeal is in the fact that everyone must work together, and in the past, great events have taken place.. the last game we played resulted in a win after throwing the dice and seeing the only result that would win it - a blank. Yeah, it usually all comes down to a dice roll, but remember - the fun is in the journey! Events like that bring people together and increase the chance this game will be played again and again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
What's most important in a coop game? Narrative, theme, tension? Team play, or to be equally playable with two as with five players?

Probably, and that must mean Lord of the Rings is the perfect coop game. You really feel a sense of accomplishment when the players reach Mordor, you'll willingly sacrifice yourself to make that happen, and you'll fear the Evil White Die. You will also immediately want to play again, win or lose. 

So why am I not rating it higher than a 7? It's certainly not because of any flaws in the game; LotR is as brilliantly designed as you would expect from Knizia. It is, however, a cooperative game, and a really linear one at that. When I play a game, I want the freedom to make grand plans and clever moves that take my opponents by surprise. I want an evolving game board, conflict and chaos.

LotR gets repetitive, fast. Perhaps playing with the expansions changes that. I'm glad I own the game, it's nice to play once in a while, but normally I'd much rather play other games. And if I do feel like playing a cooperative fantasy game, there's always Dungeons & Dragons.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I've enjoyed most of the books, the movies and being a fan of Knizia I was hoping for very good things: alas I have tried numerous times and I just can't "get into" the game (despite the beautiful production). I think this is due to 2 reasons: firstly, I find that the mechanics to the play too distracting to get into the theme.

And theme is the other obstacle: it is too rich  in that some of the unrelatable elements just become symbols and hand management issues. The linear feel doesn't help either, when War of the Ring portrays the hobbits journey (in the midst of warfare) in 2 dimensions which helps portray the story better. To better illustrate the issue of theme density, I love Knizia's Confrontation as the spacial movement and combat effects provide a more relatable theme so simply.

At least the design re-invigorated the co-operative genre, especially with the inspired rule of not showing your hand contents. In fact another good co-op (Pandemic) when played with the aforementioned rule sealed LotRs fate.

In summary, I feel this is for the fans who would love the semi-scripted and very rich narrative to relive the epic again (and again).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A co-operative game for 3-5 players. Each player represents a Hobbit and the game traces Frodo's journey in LOTR from Bag End to Mount Doom in his quest to destroy the One Ring. Some luck, hand management, cooperative planning, and foresight form the key game mechanics.

Just as in the books, the odds are stacked heavily against the players. Winning is going to be rare, but that is what makes the game memorable, and a victory doubly so. Most times, you will feel that you are confronting impossible odds, just as the Fellowship felt.

LOTR is unlikely to be enjoyed by those who have never read the books or seen the movies. While the game can feel repetitive if played multiple times within a short time span, as long as you let a few weeks pass between consecutive games, LOTR is an enjoyable game.

RATING: 6.5
First impressions after a two player game: Takes some time to get into. Neither of us had played the game before. After the first scenario we got the hang of things and made our way across the scenarios. 

We went into Mordor loaded with cards in hand and all five Gandalf cards unused, with quite some distance between ourselves and Sauron. Then bad luck struck and in three turns we were down to the last event where Sauron gets the One ring and wins.
So we lost. 

Personally I don't feel the theme as much. The life token requirement feels rather disconnected from the story, though I understand its function. The feeling opression aspect does come across well. Especially with more than three as it will be a lot harder to stave off corruption. The fact that our previous good play was undone by some rotten luck does sting a bit.
Still, we did have fun and will probably play this again. Maybe with the first expansion added.

There are some really bad translation errors in the Dutch version.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game was where the "rebirth" of Tolkein-themed board games began.  It took me by surprise, because it was unlike any game I had ever played before.  The cooperative aspects are great for playing with friends, but there is a tendency for the one most familiar or "into" the game (i.e. me) to "take charge", which can lessen the contribution of some, or at least leave them asking "What should I do now?".  The game is beautiful (beautiful art on the cards and on the boards), which makes it even more enjoyable to play (although the icons are horribly drawn and seem out of place with the rest of the great art).  It does follow the books and not the movie (another plus).  My main gripe is it's almost TOO abstract for my tastes.  It's actually sort of like a mathematical puzzle with a Lord of the Rings theme pasted onto it.  So sometimes I have to struggle to keep the theme alive when I'm deciding how many "life tokens" I should discard or whether I should move on the "feet" line or the "tree" line. Now that other LoTR boardgames have come out that I like a bit better (mainly the confrontation and WoTR), this isn't my top choice.  But it's still one of the better tolkein games out there.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Loved the books, thought the movies were pretty good (where they actually followed the plot of the books), but once again I'm disappointed by a game based on the books (that makes three so far).  This particular one was ok in the cooperation aspect, and well balanced in the tightness of resources required to reach the goal, but ultimately the players spent more time adding up shield points, figuring whether to draw or play cards based on which cards were needed in which track, and so on, than they did engrossed in the STORY LINE.  I never 'felt' like I escaped from a balrog, resisted corruption, befriended an ent, defeated a warg army, and destroyed an evil artifact - I felt like I played enough two-feet cards, star-cards, hand-cards, and sword-cards to cover the necessary card counts as they came up.  This game thus has the exact same drawbacks as the game of Beowulf, detracting just enough from its good features to make me wonder if I really want to play it a second time.  I suppose I will, some time. (Oh, and the artwork was nowhere near what I'd like to see in a game based on the books, just my opinion.) Update: It was more fun the second time, because the end was much tighter (lost by one die roll), but still has all the flaws I mentioned earlier.

RATING: 8.5
I like coops and even though this is one of the oldest (or the oldest), I like it more than most others. Even though gameplay is pretty abstract, somehow I get immersed in the struggle of the fellowship of the ring: Being in Moria and one bad event after another pops up or having no cards left and you have to get through Mordor, but you still manage to get to Mount Doom, is very rewarding. 

About the expansions: 
I do not like friend and foes (since it basically boils down to eliminating all foes, which is absolutely unthematic). 
Sauron is take-it-or-leave-it, depending if you like to have one player being the antagonist. 
Battlefields is a really excellent expansion. If you have seen enough of the base game, this is the expansion to throw in. Again, the battlefields are completely abstract, but still you get thematically immersed in fending the foes off (yes, it is rather fending off than beating them, which is the reason for it being thematic).

RATING: 9
I'm amazed at how well this game evokes the impressions and feelings that readers of the book get.  Struggling together against darkness, sacrificing here and there for the good, hoping events don't race faster than you, making timely decisions that are very difficult... the theme is not 'pasted on' as with other Knizia games -- it's in your face.  Rolling the die is something you dread -- even on the tenth, or twentieth time playing the game.  Sauron's just a little black piece when you set up the game, but an ominous specter when you're actually playing.  The co-operative aspect inspires my wife, and this never hits the table to protest.  Recommended.

RATING: 8
I am gutted to see that this game has an average rating of 6.8. Although this is not the best game I have played, it is for sure the game I have played the most. 

What this game makes it so great is that it's a cooperative game you play with two players (without additional two-player-rules) or up to five players. The cooperative element of the game is done nicely and everybody has a special role in the game.

When friends come over that are not very experienced in playing board games, this is the game to buy. Instead of defeating an disadvantaged player you can help each other and try to defeat Sauron (the board). 

My experience is that people who don't like board games seam to have changed there minds when they have played this game. It is so different to most other games where sometimes the evening ends with fights or endless discussion about the rules. When playing as a team with the same goal, fights are unlikely, and for some reason you always concur with each others interpretation of the rules.

RATING: 6

Everything you love in Pandemic is here, its tied to a really pretty theme but the timers feel a little clumsy, the victory goals a little unthematic. Essentially you play cards to advance on different tracks, they get you protection against the corrupting effect of the ring or other cards. All the time the events you face are getting worse and you are gradually being corrupted and risk falling into Sauron's hands.
Its quite fun but a little bit fiddly and I wouldn't play it over a modern co-operative.

RATING: 7
Not sure what to think after one play. I'm not one for co-op games, but this game seemed to work well since your hands are hidden. You really have to speak with everyone to find out what the best thing to do is rather than one person looking everything over and telling everyone the best thing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've grown to enjoy this game more over time. I see no reason to consider trading it away and think there is a lot to explore with just the base game. I haven't touched the expansions yet.

[b]Things I Like[/b]
:d10-1: The Tolkien/Lord of the Rings theme is done very well for what I've heard some people describe as an abstract game. I consider this game very thematic, almost dripping with theme. I appreciate the John Howe art.
:d10-2: The challenge options. The difficulty of the game is easily adjusted with the starting space of Sauron. This idea is great and is implemented very well. Everything plays the same, you simple adjust the starting "pressure" on your hobbits. New players can start off easy to get farther and become more familiar with the game. When you think you've got things figured out, just start Sauron a little closer and see how you feel.
:d10-3: The randomness present is enough to keep this game feeling "new" each time. You can't just decide to rush the main track each time and expect to win. While the challenges and resources are all the same game to game, they implement themselves in different orders and configurations each time. This keeps the challenge from getting stale.
:d10-4: Playable co-op 2-5 or solitaire with 1-5 hobbits. Speaking of challenges, they change based on the number of players. A smaller number of players results in less card availability but often having an abundance of green tokens. More players result in more cards but never enough green tokens to go around.
:d10-5: The Sauron token. I mean it's got it's own symbol here on BGG. :sauron:
:d10-6: The game rewards thoughtful planning & consideration of risk. You must carefully expend your limited resources to accomplish the quest without spending them too fast or too slowly.

[b]Neutral Comments[/b]
:d10-1: This seems to be a game that gets a lot of fringe opinions. People tend to either love it or hate it. I love it. I think many people stay away or don't like it because of its theme (can't help that), art (icons could be better, I think), or challenge.
:d10-2: Streaks of bad tile draws over a couple scenario boards will sometimes, I believe, doom you to failure no matter how well you play. I think this is rare but possible.  
:d10-3: Can be prone to an assertive personality taking charge and bossing the other players. The most experienced person should try to avoid bossing or influencing players.

[b]Summary[/b]
I really enjoy this. This will probably be received well by most Tolkien fans, abstract fans, and people who like a slower pace and careful deliberation. This is not a game suited for players who like fast, easy gameplay. Also, it's not a party game. Can be a good husband/wife game or a good game to play with children who can understand the rules and strategy.

RATING: 10
It captures a lot of the themes of LotR very well, but it also feels kind of mesozoic when compared to other cooperative games. It's somehow random without feeling dynamic. Like Frodo's journey to Mordor, this could be kind of a slog in the wrong circumstances. But as a design to step back and observe, I do admire it.

*edit* I recently traded for a new copy and have been playing some two-player games solo. It's got a lot more staying power than I originally thought, and the themes of the novel (if not the actual story beats) still ring clearly in the design. This is probably one of Knizia's best games, and I hope FFG reprints the expansions for their nice new edition.

*edit* After a few years with this game I am far more impressed by it than I was at the beginning. This is probably the best implementation of Tolkien's original story that we've seen in a board game. While a lot of it remains quite abstracted, it draws on the themes of friendship, sacrifice, and holding out against encroaching darkness so well that the feel is perfect. And lest we forget, the novel is about a journey, not a war. This game captures that perfectly, and it's a definite classic.

I have played this game many times with groups of 3-5 and every session has been great fun. There is lots of interaction with the other players as you develop a strategy for clearing each board. The best part is, beating the game is hard and feels like a real accomplishment. When you do beat it everybody wins!

RATING: 9
Les jeux coopératifs ne sont pas légion, et celui ci est sans doute le tout meilleur du genre. Du hasard (mais il faut bien renouveler les parties), une difficulté réellement présente et parfaitement calibrée… Le jeu semble insurmontable aux premières parties, puis, au fur et à mesure que l'on commence à maitriser le parcours de nos valeureux hobbits, cela devient de plus en plus facile. Il est alors temps d'augmenter le niveau de difficulté, soit en faisant démarrer Sauron plus loin, soit en acquérant une des trois extensions, qui viennent encore enrichir le jeu.

La thème est parfaitement rendu, même s'il est plus facile de penser en termes de cases et de cartes qu'en cachettes et progression. Mais il faut bien que cela reste un jeu, et pas un livre. Une totale réussite.

RATING: 7.5
I quite like this game.  I've played solitaire boardgames for years, but oddly enough didn't pick this up until Christmas, 2010.  The components in the Silver Line edition are excellent and there's a lot of game in this compact box.  

It's true that it has a Knizia "feel" to it - the heart of the design is an abstract card draw & play mechanic that doesn't have anything to do with the LOTR.  But on the other hand, as events are drawn and you progress through the game, you definitely are creating a narrative for your characters.  

Just the right amount of challenge.  I tend to lose more often than I win, but I like it that way and it's usually pretty close. I worry about re-playability.  Possibly a game I will pull off the shelf every few months for a go.

UPDATE:  I pull this out a couple times a year and still like it.  But it doesn't get more playtime than that.

RATING: 7.5
I have the much maligned FF silver line edition, and though I'd very much like the full-sized boards found in the Hasbro edition, I'm not terribly fussed. I really quite like this early co-operative game. Surprisingly thematic -- at least in 'feel,' if not mechanisms, and John Howe's iconic artwork helps matters considerably. As in most Knizia offerings, the mechanisms are elegant and flow quite well. At it's core, LOTR is a hand management game where you're simply moving up different tracks, but the often punishing nature of the events (a string of bad things followed by temporary relief) replicates the 'hanging by the skin of your teeth' feel of the LOTR.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Really quite interesting from a design perspective. Not only is the integration with theme the best I've seen from RK, but the way the game is balanced is just masterful. If it were simply as matter of clever design, however, this game wouldn't garner such a high rating from me.

The key words here are tense and fun. LotR is the kind of game that encourages great table talk, largely due to the way the game is integrated with the theme, but also because it is so cooperative and so tense.

I am concerned that, at some point in the future, the decision making algorithm for players will become transparent, robbing the game of its ability to create interesting choices for players. But thus far, that hasn't been that big of a problem. Even if you are making "optimal" decisions as a team, the level of randomness still manages to inject an exciting level of tension and surprises do occur.

I will play this game pretty much any time. Although I thrive on competitive games (even though I am not competitive - I'm equally happy to lose, I just happen to like competitive game designs), I enjoy the change of pace and the feeling of cameradrie created by LotR. This could be a 10, but I'm still worried about replayability. I'll just have to wait and see.

Update: Definitely worthy of at least a 9. This game is just peachy. Further Update: After a dozen plays  plays, I can vouch for the replayability here. Lord of the Rings is just an excellent cooperative game. I suppose, with boorish gamers, there is the risk of someone attempting to "quarterback" the game and pushing for particular decisions, but we haven't had this problem. We all enjoy a little huddle where-in the pros and cons of a decision are discussed, and then it is up to the *player* to actually make the decision. Excellent, excellent game - we always play with the Friends and Foes expansion.

RATING: 7
Great artwork, great gameplay.  I always enjoy when this comes to the table. The sense of each player sacrificing to achieve the common goal is consistent with the LotR theme and forces a lot of tough group decision making and, from my experience, helps avoid the leader issue common in co-ops. 

The game loses replay value, though, when you've played the game enough to learn what cards need to be stockpiled for future scenarios. At that point, it becomes a matter of making sure everyone is prepared for what you know is coming - and this is very INconsistent with the LotR theme.  At that point, the game boils down to hoping you don't get on a nasty run of event tiles in each scenario.

RATING: N/A
I like this game for the theme and probably from nostalgia since it was my first board game to reach beyond the old standards like Monopoly.

The actual gameplay is VERY random based on drawing from a bag.  As you are going through the game, one draw at specific points in the game can change it from an easy victory to utter defeat.  For whatever reason I love trying to complete the quest and working together, but how to win is pretty straight forward after playing a couple times.  You will also have those unlucky games where you get destroyed because of bad luck with nothing you could do about it.  

If you like co-operative games and enjoy the LOTR story and world then this is something you can think about.  But for how long this game can take, the random draw factor, and the so-so mechanics I wouldn't suggest this to anyone else and would find something else.

RATING: 8.5
You have to give credit for innovation on this game.  The cooperative nature of the game, playing against the Dark Lord, is great!  Game play is nail-biting and nerve-racking, especially in Mordor.    The more that I play this game, the more that I am impressed by the balance of the cards, mechanisms and abilities within the game.  For instance, with two players, keeping enough cards in your hand is difficult, but getting life tokens is easy.  With four players, there are usually enough cards to go around, but it is really tough to get all of the life tokens that you need.  Very few games have the tense moments that this game brings about.  The desparation attempts to keep the fellowship alive while crawling through Mordor do an excellent job of mirroring the atmosphere of the books.

RATING: 8.5
I'm not a fan of abstract games. This game is abstract. I don't like high luck in games, and this is filled with luck. I really shouldn't like this game - it's Knizia, who's games are often way abstract, and I also don't like the artwork very much at all (sorry John Howe fans - I know he's considered one of the best). But...there is just something about this game that I love! Really, your just hoping to get the right cards at the right time, and if you don't, your screwed. So why do I like it so much? I don't know if I can put my finger on it. It flows along at a very nice pace, its incredibly foreboding and fun to watch Sauron come closer and closer to the hobbits  - and vice versa, the way each hobbit has to obtain rings and hearts and such is a nice mechanic, there are some rough choices to have to make - which is nice, and in the end, if you win it is truly a high-five slappin moment because lord knows you thought you were gonna lose for sure. Its one of those games where I truly understand why some people don't like it, but to me, it really brings players together in a true co-op feeling that you are all really working together toward a common goal. 

Here's what to do: on a stormy day, get together a few friends or perhaps just you and your lover,  bring up the lord of the rings soundtrack, put some logs on the fire, pull out this game, and have an awesome time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I respect this game for what it is, arguably the first modern co-op game ever made (and for many years, the only modern co-op game).  If I was into hobby board games back in the early 2000's, I don't know what I would have thought of the game.  I didn't play it until late 2014, where I can't help but compare it to newer titles such as Pandemic, Eldritch Horror, Robinson Crusoe, Mage Knight, etc.

At it's core, Lord of the Rings is a pretty straight-forward game: collect and use cards to advance up a track(s) to complete a conflict from the books (Moria, Helm's Deep, Shelob's Lair, and finally Mordor) before the ring-bearer becomes completely corrupted.  

My major complaint with the game is how random the story tiles can be, and how there's little-to-no means to mitigate a bad string of event draws.  It's really frustrating to get 3 events in a row on the first player's turn, which unfortunately happens more often than it should for me.

Furthermore, the base game seems to have a dominant strategy to just burn through the main activity track as quickly as possible, in hopes that events won't trigger, which makes me wonder about replay value.  

As much as I love Lord of the Rings, there are tons of games I would rather play solo/co-op over this.

RATING: 6
Despite all the claims, to me this game just doesn't feel very thematic. Instead, it feels like you're flipping over tiles, rolling dice, and trying to find the "hand" or "boots" card amongst the group at the right time. The fact that you can't show everyone what's in your hand doesn't change the fact that it's basically playing from 1 large hand of cards that has just been split amongst the 2-5 players. It doesn't feel like there are many decisions here; you're just playing your hand out and hoping the right tile/dice rolls come up for you.

I much prefer Shadows Over Camelot; even Vanished Planet is probably better. I'd almost drop this to a 5, but will wait to play it more first (but that may be a while!)

RATING: 5
Reiner Knizia's games are often accused of having "pasted on" themes but this is one I think is incredibly thematic.  The game follows the plot of the story of the Lord of the Rings which I quite enjoy.  The characters, items, and events of the game mirror the books quite well and make for a very fine setting for this game.

That said, I found the game a bit boring and very dry.  There just seems to be one thing missing to make this a "game" to me.  The way it is just feels like micromanagement.  I know a lot of cooperative board games involve a lot of micromanagement and risk assessment but they also have something that makes them fun.  This game seems like more work than fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There was a time when I would have scored this a 9 or a 10. First of all, it captures the essence of the books perfectly in gameplay and in art. Secondly, it's a cooperative game (the first I ever played), and it works, for the most part. Lastly, you can make it as challenging as you like, so it's not an easy victory ever. To win, you really need to sacrifice members of the Fellowship. On the bad side, it does take a while, and some people in my gaming group have declared that they "hate this game". Since that admission, it's been largely unplayed. Perhaps its most major fault, and the fault of most co-op games, is that the experienced players can tend to run things and the inexperienced ones tend to just do as they're told. If the experienced ones let the inexperienced do as they like, it's frustrating, because they won't really do well. Conversely, how much fun is it to have someone play your game for you? You need the right crowd for this to work.

RATING: 6.5
Excellent co-operative game that beat the rest of the pack to market by years and yet is still better than most of them (I'm looking at you, Shadows Over Camelot). It's less elegant and less strategic than Pandemic, still the gold standard, but the separate conflict boards do a good job conveying a narrative progression. There's a story to it. Much as I usually dislike dice, the threat die works perfectly, adding an element of risk (do you gamble and hope not to lose too much?) and also genuine drama. Very good.

If I ever had much cause to play cooperative games then this would be in regular rotation, but as it is I can't justify holding on to it.

RATING: 6.5
[Based on a few plays] I have played both with and without the expansions.  Really I do not mind this game.  It is a decent enjoyable game to play. RATING: 7.5
[FFG 2001 edition w/ the Black Gate card] {Fantasy, Novel; co-operative, hand management, dice-rolling, player elimination; optimal 5p} LoTR is a vintage cooperative game. It is very thematic and full of player interaction. Players are very engaged in the game. The art work is a classic but the life tokens and shields look childish.

It pleases the fans of The Lord of the Rings very much. I think this game will be even lovelier if the whole fellowship is involved.  I really would like to be Aragon.  Thus, I like the expansions quite much.

It's not easy to teach the rule due to the fiddly rules. There are a few confusions in the rules, it's better to read the FAQs before playing.

I usually left my friends decide how to play LoTR: either seriously or causally. LoTR is challenging when played seriously where players to cooperative really well in order to destroy the ring. Of course, when playing casually (allowing players to leak excessive information), it is also fun.  No matter how you play it, it's very challenging to place Sauron at 10 to start the game.

I love the original and fabulous design of LoTR.  It drives a balance between rushing to finish a scenario and gathering resources with the collection of life tokens without destroying the theme. In addition, the mixture of good and evil tiles is just right - neither too hard nor too easy.  In addition, different aspects and levels of consideration demand strong cooperation.

[Expansions] The expansions are good, but they are not musts though I bought them all :P  Besides, playing LoTR with all expansions will make the game more complex and longer, though it's still manageable and interesting.

[Variants] The 2 extra cards are not necessary: they just make the game much easier without increasing teamwork / competition unless the official variant is used. The 23 new dark event tiles is a nice add-on but allowing players to reject tile may make the game easier. It would be more challenging if a player can only reject at most 2 tiles in his turns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.5
An absolute masterpiece and unique design. The experience is better if you know Tolkien's background.It's cooperative play, and it works wonderfully; but maybe it's not for everyone.
The expansions add a lot of variety.You can decide on several parameters to set-up the difficulty : starting space for Sauron, one or two or all the expansions, special blue cards (The One Ring, Watchful Peace, The Black Gate).
Some of my best gaming memories are related to this game. I remember some terrific games in unusual dark places with players able to add some role-play, in a 'Tolkien-esque' way.
My suggestions for a strong and flavorful experience : find a nice room where you can diminish the lights; use a few candles ; do not forget the music (soundtrack of LOTR movies, etc.)(first comments made in 2003 - updated May 2012)

RATING: 6
It's really hard for me to get cooperative games to the table, and this one is even worse, because there's always that one person that's willing to play [i]almost[/i] anything, but isn't willing to cross the line to a game with this theme.  It's like "I may be playing board games, but I'm not a geek, and Lord of the Rings is only for geeks."  Uh-huh.  Go back to playing Xbox then.

That said, I find that more than half the time I've played this, it ends up with one person dominating the game, and giving instructions to others - either because they want to win, or because there's some other people so clueless (or disinterested) that they have to be told what to do.  That is doubleplusungood.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The Lord of the Rings game fits my group well.  We all like the books and movies, and the cooperative nature of the game goes along with the theme perfectly.  When you have a nemesis or two in your gaming group that you are always competing against, it feels good to use their brain power for good instead of evil every once in a while.  Also, it doesn't bother me that, for example, Galadriel and Aragorn are functionally identical in the game.  They save you in two different instances, and since I tend to internally fill in the flavor as we play, that works for me.  Our games have tended to balance well, that is we end up struggling through Mordor, not sure if we are going to make it or not.  Add in the fact that the game is easily handicapped for experience, and you have a solid, engaging, exciting game.

I upgraded this to a perfect 10 after 30 or so plays.  A lot of that has to do with how much my kids love the game.  We have had many close finishes, complete with extreme amounts of cheering and groaning.  Great game!

RATING: 7.5
It's essentially a series of trial-and-error situations.  Once you've learned the solutions for each path, you just have to hope that you'll be lucky enough to draw the correct tiles to solve it in time.  There really aren't enough interesting decisions to be made.  You need to do some risk management, but the results of your choices are dependent on memory and luck more than strategy.

The presentation is wildly inconsistent, ranging from the gorgeous boards to the babyish tokens.  Pick an aesthetic and stick with it.

I don't mind it when I'm actually in the middle of a game, but I rarely am in the mood to break it out.

UPDATE: I traded my copy in because I got a good price, but I'd definitely add it to my collection again if I found it at a garage sale or thrift store.  I never was able to give it a go with other people -- only played solo -- which might be a big reason why it never grabbed me.

UPDATE 2: Got another copy.  I'm enjoying it more this time around.  Yes, it's a series of puzzles, but the puzzles are fun and do a really good job of hitting the highlights of the theme and narrative of the story.

RATING: 7
Once I had it organized the way I like, the setup was all the quicker (usually its long set up times that discourage me from pulling a game out).
I wrestled with whether or not to get the Silverline Edition because the expansions are not compatible with it but, as of now, I will say that I love the art much more than what I see for the other editions.  The art is good enough to draw me into the theme.
Gameplay is tough. Lots of luck involved for sure, but it still feels balanced the "right" way. After all, the chances of defeating Sauron should be slim.
Good about the game: Feels thematic.
Not good about the game: Playing solo can be challenging because I need to recall the cards in hand for all the characters (3). Also, even though this isn't the fault of the game, the set-up time keeps me from bringing it out a lot which, in turn, means I need to refresh myself with the rules every time I play.

RATING: 8
I get why some hate this game.  I can understand how someone would see this as dry and abstract.

However, I think the game is great fun and what I find most amazing is that such an abstract game can feel so much like an adventure.  The game really asks you to use a lot of imagination.  Playing a card that simply shows a picture of the Phial of Galadrial and has a poorly drawn foot in the corner doesn't immediately evoke the feeling of finding your way through Shelob's Lair.  

But if I take a little time to create the story (in your head or out loud) and savor it for just a split second I find that I can create a version of my favorite story.

I believe you would almost have to love the book or movie to really enjoy the game.  Without creating the story it is just a simple game of efficiency.

The game is also very open to scaling it to your own sweet spot.  You can start Sauron closer or farther, add the Foes (I haven't yet), or play with other hobbits.  

Most of the components are excellent.  I love that they went with the John Howe artwork.  The figures are nice.  

I hate the event tiles.  They are completly incrongruous with the rest of the beautiful art.

I have only played this as a two-player (with my wife who also loves LoTR).  I can see that this could be more fun with more.

I understand why some wouldn't like it, but for me it is not only the best cooperative around it is one of my favorite story/adventure games.

Update: I can no longer say this the best co-op around. Pandemic is just too good. But, Lord of the Rings, you will always be my first and no game can ever take that away.




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 4
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 7
My wife and I enjoy this, but it isn't something we pull out all the time. The unrelenting progress of doom is all there in the game, but the relief or the catastrophe that should be the endgame is lacking. It's something that we always find anticlimactic. So, how, in a  game, do you convey the utter disaster of Sauron reclaiming the Ring or the fantastic sense of actually having saved the world? My guess is that, unfortunately, this is about as close as it gets.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
The Lord of the Rings Board Game is one of the early examples of coop games. As far as I know it's the first full coop euro game, and for that it should receive merit. Today though we can see the many design flaws in this one. It features player eliminiation which is a no go for a coop. It can be unwinnable even if you play it perfect. While it tells you a story, it does tell the same one over and over again, and a story we all know far too well on top of that.

As I said, this game should receive some merit. But it aged very bad.

RATING: 7
One play -  I'm almost scared to play this again because I had such a fun time playing it the first time I am not sure I can ever recover it.  I actually felt like I was a character in the novel.  I'm not a big LOTR fan, nor do I get into the spirit of roleplaying.  This was an absolute blast.  Reminds me of golfing, i.e. a fun way to get frustrated.

More plays - Its still fun.  Lots of it.   The golfing analogy holds true, you are playing against the course, and the course is designed to screw with you.  Really looking forward to playing with the expansions, once I figure out how to crush Sauron.  Muhahahaha!  I'm not a big cooperative person, so this 9 is probably a 10 if you like the coop thing.

Hmm... this hasn't aged well for me... i can only see it as a two player game for potential "coaching" problems with players with different levels of experience.  The game doesn't play you... you are just a pawn of "the coach".  Plus, if someone playing is making a drastic bad decision, I feel like I should "coach" him to make a better move, because his horrible decision takes the fun out of it for me.  

Inherent problem of the co-op genre for me I guess. 

The newer coop games leave this one in the dust.  Space alert and pandemic would be my coop choice over this every single time and twice on sunday.

(Several plays pre-Geek)

Captures the spirit of the books well - it doesn't necessarily feel like you are fighting orcs or creeping through Moria, but it *does* feel like you're struggling together to accomplish something in the face of the threat of corruption, possibly sacrificing one or more of your party to do this.

A little bit long and a little bit fiddly, but good fun.  Cooperative play is a nice change of pace.

RATING: 7.3
Takes the narrative tropes of an "American" game (like an [GAMEID=15987] or [GAMEID=10547]) and marries them to Euro mechanics. To me this is a really interesting reminder that there's nothing sacred about dice and tables when it comes to scripting game narratives.

Interesting to note, though, that you only get prose narrative in this game through the Event track, and Events are generally a [i]bad[/i] thing, to be avoided. The opportunities on the board correspond to events from the novels, but have no narrative associated with them in the game. So, the better you succeed at the game, the [i]less[/i] theme you get. This seems like a major missed opportunity.

Also: Little white cones? Not the best choice. Big chunky plastic Eye of Sauron? More like it.

RATING: 1
Simply AWEFUL game.  The DEFINITION of pasted on theme.  Frankly, I'd have enjoyed it a lot more if they'd stripped the LOTR theme off and made it a purely abstract cooperative game.  

It's not a game, it's a puzzle disguised to look like a game.  Oh, and if the random card draws are against you, it is often impossible to win.  Combine this with the fact that players can be eliminated, and we have a trifecta of suck.  No wait, we've passed three sucks a while back.  What's the equivalent of a Trifecta with five things?

1> It murders what should be an excellent theme!
2> The only decision to make is, Do I do the only option, or do I choose to Lose?  This makes it not a game, but a puzzle where you put the pieces in in order. 
3> Randomness results in a frequently unwinnable non-game.  Because often the pieces you get don't fit in the puzzle there, so you die.
4> Crap plastic bits that look like crap, combined with crap artwork, means it isn't even pretty to look at.  If the art on the cards were better, or if the player pieces were detailed rather than identical molded plastic, it might distract you from the lack of actual depth.
5> Oh yeah, it's pretty easy for someone to F up in the first or second encounter and be eliminated!  HUZZAH! Oh what fun!  Sit and wait for a hour while your friends continue to suck their way to failure in a rather ugly non-game!  

All in all, this one is pretty aweful.

It's put me off Reiner Knizia.

RATING: 10
Lord of the Rings is a co-operative game where the object is to destroy the Ring while surviving the corrupting influence of Sauron. Each player plays one of the Hobbits in the fellowship, each of which has a unique power. The game is played on a number of boards: the Master board indicates both the physical progress of the fellowship across Middle Earth and the corrupting influence of Sauron on the hobbits, and a number of scenario boards which detail the events and adventures of particular locations. Progression across the boards is determined by playing cards (many of which represent the characters and items of Middle Earth), and the effects of corruption are represented by a special die. The game is lost if the ring-bearer is overcome by Sauron, or won if the ring is destroyed by throwing it into the volcanic fires of Mount Doom.

RATING: 7
LOTR is a precursor to other co-op games. This game really elevated the co-op genre in the eyes of gamers, with Pandemic sealing the deal much later.  Even if the game is more than 15 years old, it clearly shows many elements that are still widely used in other co-op games such as mechanism of "evil/disaster/calamity occurs ahead of each player action" In the case of LOTR, having to flip over multiple event tiles to trigger bad events really elevates the tension and difficulty of the game. Unfortunately, that also brings a heavier dose of luck into the game play. I particularly enjoy how thematic the game is. Many elements on the game boards, cards and mechanisms really meshes well with how the story progresses in the books. Knizia can do theme!

Still, the atmosphere of playing a co-op is always thick with tension and immersive. I find it a very good change of pace from your standard competitive games. I have never been a huge fan of co-op games, but this genre is slowly warming up to me as the heavier FFG games fall wayside.

RATING: 8.5
A superb achievement from Dr. Knizia.  Cooperative gameplay the is best touch (other than a very well-executed theme, of course).  Deep, immersive, gorgeous.  Only flaw, IMHO, is just how much luck is involved.  The event tiles are so powerful that no amount of collaborative strategy can save you if you draw poorly, and likewise you can be rash buffoons yet still succeed if you draw well.  However, the play is still mostly fun even if you have one of the statistical outlier tile-draw game sessions.

RATING: 9
Very unique, even among cooperative games.  Competing together against the board can be a welcome change, especially if you just got done squabbling over a rules ambiguity in a high-conflict game.  LotR is my favorite cooperative game, as it gets players immersed in the narrative in a way in which other games ([gameid=30549], [gameid=7240]) don't always manage.  My favorite example of a well-themed euro, this has lots of little nods to the storyline, but nicely packaged into the game system so that they don't feel tacked-on.  Has surprising amount of replay value, especially for a cooperative game.  Everyone should try it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
This was my very first co-op game and also the first board game I played solo. 

I maintain that despite the mechanics looking abstract on the surface, this does an great job of capturing its source material. Whereas other LotR based games often focus on combat, this concentrates on the Fellowship's journey to Mount Doom and does so through literal progress tracks that can be filled up by using items, enlisting the help of allies etc. 

The only problem is that the videogame esque linear progression through several distinct "levels" limits replayability somewhat.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is a strange one. Move pawns up and down a track to collect tokens with meaningless symbols on them, until the point at which a completely arbitrary event either wins or loses you the round. Then move the pawns to another arcane board and do it all again. It's really bizarre.

In the interest of full disclosure, I have to admit that I tricked myself into liking this when I first bought it. The world was in the grip of a Peter Jackson inspired Lord of the Rings mania and I sort of got swept along. That's not to say it's a bad game, it does exactly what it sets out to do. The players have to pool their resources together and use them to navigate an obstacle course. As one of the first co-op boardgames that didn't completely suck, it was actually a pretty neat experience. The reason my feelings about it have changed is that so many better co-ops have been released over the past few years. When you look at how well games like Pandemic, Arkham Horror, and Battlestar Galactica relate their mechanisms to theme...the Knizia game just seems silly and disconnected.

I gave this an 8 back in it's heyday. I still consider it one of the more important games in the evolution of the hobby, but I don't think playing it is as fun as it once was.

RATING: 10
Love the requirement for true cooperation, the ease of difficulty level adjustment, the constant pressure on the Fellowship.  It has some luck, yes, and some sameness from game to game, but overall I think Knizia succeeded brilliantly.

Also, I see a lot of remarks about the lack of theme, but that view is rubbish; the theme is strong, but expressed in an unusual way.

After many plays, I must concede that the base game alone is too linear, and that the "main path rush" is just too strong (which reduces replayability even more).  But that's what expansions are for.

RATING: 8
I've played this a lot. It's a little abstract but all the bits and boards are really nice and the artwork is great. I recommend buying it used.

I've been playing two players with the Friends & Foes expansion. So far we've made it as far as 48. The last game we barely made it past 30 and the last few steps were grueling. I kept thinking we had lost and then we came up with a way to stay in the game three or four times.

We finally won the base game with three players and haven't played in a while. After reading the strategy tips the game is obviously WAY harder for two players when playing with Friends & Foes. So... we need to line up some more players before we'll try it again.

Old comments: The last game we played we got hammered in Moria. We got like 7 event tiles in a row. I was trying to teach a friend how to play so this made it rough. Ugh. We kept with it and got to 37. Yikes. So this is a fun game, but bad luck can make it rough with little room for skill/strategy to help. Also, I wish the theme and gameplay were a little closer together. I want to feel more like we're battling those battles etc. Still, I like the game a lot. It's fun working together.

RATING: N/A
Card driven fantasy co-op.  Play cards from hand that match symbols along primary and alternate tracks of each quest leading to Mount Doom.  Get help from starting Elron items and the timely arrival of Gandalf.  The corruption track draws the fellowship into the clutches of Sauron.  Event tokens and card draws lead to very random game results.  Decisions are straightforward.  Light and nostalgic.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
A co-operative game, destroy the Ring while surviving the corrupting influence of Sauron. The Master board indicates both the physical progress of the fellowship across Middle Earth and the corrupting influence of Sauron. Scenario boards detail the events of particular locations. Progress by playing cards which represent the characters and items of Middle Earth. 

Awards
2000 Spiel des Jahres Special Award for "Literary Game"

RATING: 10
Enthralling, if you're into the theme. Very immersive, especially with the expansions.

29/05/2017: The problem I have with my copy is that it's the Silver Line edition from FFG and they didn't ever bring out the expansions for it. I really enjoyed LoTR with Sauron and Friends and Foes, but without those I would play it happily but I'm also played out on it. This game also has the problem of later co-ops that have really superseded it in terms of mechanics (Forbidden Desert) and epic narrative (Pandemic Legacy). If I'm going to play a 5-player 1-hour co-op it's probably going to be Forbidden Desert at the moment, and I'm not the biggest co-op fan due to the alpha-player problem, so I just can't see a space for this in my collection. I still highly recommend it for fans of the books/movies as a way to engage with that world as a member of the fellowship (rather than as some sort of hand of God like in War of the Ring).

My copy is has only been played twice, the other plays were on a friend's big-box copy from back in the early 2000s

RATING: 7.25
I was surprised by this.  I bought this game about 2 years ago based on a few reviews but mostly because of the fantastic series of review/play-through videos by Grudunza.

After the purchase, I began hearing negative reviews about it.  The refrain was that, 'It becomes repetitive."  Others stated that newer cooperative games are much better.  I shelved it but because I love most things that have to do with The Lord of the Rings, my eye always lingered on the box as I decided what to play.

I recently pulled it out and played a number of games solo.  I have missed a wonderful gaming experience for these last few years by ignoring it.  It drew me in immediately and I wanted to share the experience with my family, having them play also.  

The feel of the books and movies is woven throughout the game.  The tension level is high.  The difficulty level draws you into wanting to give it another go after your most recent failed quest.

In addition, I like the cooperative rule of not having your cards splayed out for every other player to see as is the mechanic in most other coop games.  You must talk to the other players instead of only gazing at their cards.  This seemingly small element adds tension because of trust issues.  You think, "Can I trust my Fellowship members, or are they tanking it to force me to play my cards while saving theirs?"

Though I see the game may get repetitive, the expansions all add variety and new ways to strategically move through the quest.  I want them all! :)

RATING: 8
I agree that it is not remotely like D&D (as that was the designers intent) but i would also argue that the new ways that have been used in this game to represent *all* the elements of fantasy adventure are no more artificial that all the D&D cloning going on within the dungeoncrawl genre. In fact what he has done is capture all the elements but with mechanics that intentionally bare no resemblance to the current conventions. He does have maps, but intentionally different. He does have character advancement, but intentionally different. etc etc.

We are so caught up in redoing D&Ds artificial conventions that something radically different (but just as realistic at portraying the fantasy environment) comes off as abstract when it is not. Dungeoncrawl is not a theme but it is a body of "D&D like" mechanics. This is a very difficult thing for some to see.

This really is one of Knizia's greatest achievements because it is a slap in the face of what we considered dungeoncrawl and really makes us think about it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]MOST ENJOYABLE.[/b]

This fantastic game was my first geeky geek geek game for geeks that I bought while entering the second phase of my boardgaming life. Knizia's LoTR was a solid waypoint on my road to BGG. I do like this spin on Tolkien's mega-quest, but I'd be the first to hold up my hand and say that if I'd been tasked with creating a LOTR boardgame, this is not what I would have produced - that's why I'm not a world-renowned game designer!

The players all take the role of hobbits working their way to Mt.Doom to destroy the One Ring. You know the plot.
Cardplay powers the game through different gameboards representing Shelob's Lair, Moria, and Mordor, etc.
The theme is good, the boards are cool, and the co-op system is fairly unique. There is some talk of the game being slightly abstract, and I can see that POV. I admire this Knizia effort, and I do find myself thoroughly immersed in the theme, loving the artwork. It also helps muchly if the whole group are huge LOTR fans.
We have only beaten this once in twenty games: it's tough!

The game seems to split groups right down the middle, with those liking, and those disliking it, IMO.
I've never found myself being "played" by the game, but others retain this memory of the experience. Maybe if I'd played this game to death, analysed every failed attempt, argued over the use of everyone's cards, I would have tired of it, knowing when to "hide" or "fight," but that's simply not been the case for me.

On the subject of expansions, Battlelfields is a marvellous addition to the base game in that it increases the epic feel of the adventure, with the entire fellowship involved at last. I don't like the flowchart presentation of the boards, but after including it in our games I forgot all about that as we struggled to complete our mission against the forces of evil. The expansion grows the game  decision tree and increases your immersion in the quest.

BTW, Reiner, the rulesbook is a complete dog, needing a rewrite badly; I'm sure this has put off casual game players. There is a reason why you can pick up a copy of this game on eBay for zip any day of the week, and it's not overproduction!

THEME IMMERSION TIP: replace the hobbit pawns and Sauron with painted miniatures. If you have the time to do so, I'd highly recommend it.

[b]THE GANDALF:[/b]

1. Glorious artwork.
2. Multi-board quest locations.
3. Cooperative.
4. Rich theme.
5. Narrative-forming gameplay.

[b]THE SARUMAN:[/b]

1. Abstract.
2. Terrible rulebook.
3. Tough.
4. Needs expansions to shine like the Star of Elendil.

NB. Check out my rules for using Gollum as one of the players. IT'S MY PRECIOUS!

I own ALL of the expansions.

RATING: 10
:sauron: Extremely clever game design. I own the 2nd Edition Hasbro version.

:sauron: 11 years late getting this game, but as good as, if not better than other co-op's like Pandemic.

:sauron: Some folk say the newer FFG edition is far better. But just like the book, or a fine wine, the Hasbro edition has matured with age. I tried to play the FFG edition once, but after setting up the game, I just thought - no thanks, boxed it back up, and returned to my beloved original copy.

RATING: 5
Кооперативная игра от Книция - много математики, есть кубик (случай), очень сложная для прохождения - в результате от удачи зависит многое, хотя по-началу так не кажется: выпадение на кубике непотребств, выход карточек сценария во время боевой части игры, в результате от игроков требуется выбрать себе лидера, отдать ему бразды и тихо смотреть, как он пытается добраться до Ородруина. 

Возможно, игра входит в топ100 из-за дополнений, но их у меня нет.

RATING: 7
Feels a bit more abstract than other, newer cooperative games, so I always thought that everyone's praises of how thematic the game is were a little funny. Still, this is a great game and a relevant one. The quality of the production, which is important to me, is top-notch. Everything looks great.

Own the Friends & Foes expansion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
4/9/07 - LOTR is hard to compare to other games because it is so unlike any other game.  Don't listen to comparisons with other "cooperative" games (like Arkham Horror).  They all suck hard and this one rocks.  I think what's best about this game is that I can be on the same team as my friends, rather than having to compete against them, and that alone skyrockets this game to the top.  When you play this game, your actions are so intimately intertwined with your allies' that you begin to feel bad when you have to stop to draw cards because you feel like you've let them down.  I have played this game in excess of 15 times and its just as great as ever.
---
Lord of the Rings is one of the best games ever made.  In it, you get to be part of a Dream Team of hobbits, braving the corrupting influence of Sauron in an effort to cast the One Ring of power back into the firey bowels of Mt. Doom.  When I play, I usually die somewhere in the middle of the ashen wastes of Gorgoroth, having endured so much pain and agony at the hands of evil, only to return the Ring to Sauron's hand and plummet all of Middle Earth into Shadow.
